Post-conviction relief denied at, Petition granted by, Decision reached on appeal by State v. Soto-Fong, 2007 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1116 (2007)
Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Soto-Fong, 2010 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 467 (2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County No. CR-39599. Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County. The Honorable Robert B. Buchanan, Judge.
State v. McCrimmon, 187 Ariz. 169, 927 P.2d 1298, 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 120 (Ariz., 1996)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Pima County (Arizona) convicted defendant of multiple counts of murder and robbery. He was sentenced to death on each of the murder counts and to terms of years on all the other counts. A direct, automatic appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was 17-years old when he committed the offenses for which he was later convicted. The defendant argued that he was misidentified as the offender. On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions and the sentences. The court held that the trial court did not err in refusing to exclude portions of defendant's witness' testimony regarding identity because the statements were not sufficiently against the declarant's penal interest such that a reasonable person would not have made them. Moreover, defendant opened the door by introducing the topic on direct examination of the witness. Thus, cross-examination extended to all matters covered by direct examination. In addressing the death penalty issue, the court set aside the especially heinous, cruel, or depraved killing, an aggravating factor found in Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(6). However, the aggravating factors of pecuniary gain, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(5), and multiple homicides, Ariz. Rev. Stat. 13-703(F)(8), were affirmed. The court also found that defendant's age was a statutory mitigator. Nonetheless, because the mitigating evidence was de minimis the court held that a remand for resentencing was not necessary.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences.

State v. Dickens

Writ of certiorari denied Dickens v. Arizona, 522 U.S. 920, 118 S. Ct. 311, 139 L. Ed. 2d 240, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 6137 (1997)
Habeas corpus proceeding at Dickens v. Ryan, 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 16131 (9th Cir. Ariz., Aug. 3, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Yuma County No. C-18454. Appeal from the Superior Court in Yuma County. The Honorable Tom C. Cole, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought a review of the judgment by the Superior Court in Yuma County (Arizona), which convicted him of two counts of felony murder, conspiracy to commit armed robbery, and armed robbery. The trial judge sentenced defendant to death on the felony murder counts and to fourteen years' imprisonment on the conspiracy and armed robbery convictions. The state filed a cross appeal regarding the jury instruction on flight or concealment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for his participation with a co-conspirator in felony murder and armed robbery. On appeal the court held that (1) defendant's prior statement was admissible because his ability to understand, respond, or think clearly was not impaired, (2) the allowance of the motion to reopen was proper because the state gained no unfair tactical advantage, (3) the exclusion of specific act evidence was proper because defendant did not provide the trial judge with a specific offer of proof regarding other acts, (4) the failure to strike jurors for cause was not erroneous because the jurors' potential bias was unknown to the parties or the judge during voir dire, (5) photographs were admissible because they were not unduly prejudicial or inflammatory, (6) evidence that defendant obtained the murder weapon was probative of motive, intent, and plan, (7) the physical evidence supported the robbery and felony murder, (8) the jury instructions were correct statements of law, (9) the trial judge's death eligibility findings were not inconsistent with the verdict because the aggravating factor of cruelty existed beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for armed robbery and conspiracy to commit armed robbery and the sentences imposed.

State v. DePiano


Supreme Court No. CR-95-0099-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


187 Ariz. 27; 926 P.2d 494; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 96; 224 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 34



September 5, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied January 21, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 639.


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeals No. 1 CA-CR 92-1855. Maricopa County No. CR 91-92829. Appeal from the Superior Court of Arizona in Maricopa County. The Honorable Maurice Portley, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One    Ariz.    ,     P.2d     (1995).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court of Arizona in Maricopa County SENTENCE MODIFIED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One VACATED IN PART


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was charged with two counts of intentional or knowing child abuse under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-3623(B)(1). The trial court convicted the appellant of all charges and sentenced her to two consecutive 17-year prison terms that required her to serve the full 34 years before release. The Court of Appeals (Arizona) affirmed her convictions and sentence. The appellant challenged her sentence.


OVERVIEW: The appellant had been, by all accounts, a very good mother. Shortly after her second child was born, her husband deserted her. Thereafter, she began dating. When she later broke up with her boyfriend, she moved out of the house they shared, although she had no income and no place to live. She was depressed, upset, and disillusioned. She attempted to commit suicide and infanticide by asphyxiation, but was interrupted by an alert neighbor. All survived and no one was injured. The appellant was convicted and alleged on appeal that her sentence for two counts of intentional or knowing child abuse was cruel and unusual under the federal and state constitutions. The court held that the appellant's sentence was not cruel and unusual. However, the court concluded that pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4037(B), the sentence was excessive in light of the circumstances of the particular crime. The court, therefore, reduced the appellant's sentence to the minimum statutory mitigated term of 24 years.


OUTCOME: The court vacated that part of the court of appeals' opinion that related to the cruel and unusual punishment issue. The court affirmed the appellant's convictions, but reduced the 17-year term of imprisonment for each count to 12 years. The sentences in all other respects were affirmed.

State v. Miller


Supreme Court No. CR-93-0557-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


186 Ariz. 314; 921 P.2d 1151; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 78; 220 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 63



July 11, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied February 24, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 1339.


PRIOR HISTORY: Pima County No. CR-38383. Appeal from the Superior Court of Arizona in Pima County. The Honorable Lawrence H. Fleischman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The case was an automatic and direct appeal under Ariz. R. Crim. P. 26.15, 31.2(b) and Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4031 of a judgment of the Superior Court of Arizona in Pima County, which convicted defendant of premeditated first-degree murder and kidnapping and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and kidnapping and sentenced to death. The court affirmed the convictions and sentences. The court held that the search warrant clearly indicated the basis for the police officer's belief that incriminating evidence would be found at defendant's residence and in his car. The court found that defendant's statements were voluntary because he thought he was helping himself when he provided the highly incriminating evidence. The search warrant authorized the police to detain defendant while the warrant was being executed but that the officers' actions went beyond detention and constituted an illegal arrest of defendant without probable cause. The court held that defendant's statements were not the fruit of the illegal arrest because the confession was voluntary, the proximity between the arrest and the statement was not immediate, and defendant understood he could end the interview and return home. The court found that both the heinous and depraved finding and the cruelty finding were proper aggravating factors and that defendant's intoxication was not a mitigating factor because it did not impair his ability to drive.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that convicted defendant of premeditated first-degree murder and kidnapping and sentenced him to death. The court held that the trial court did not err in its balancing of the aggravating and mitigating factors for sentencing.

State v. Hyde


Supreme Court CR-92-0289-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


186 Ariz. 252; 921 P.2d 655; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 75; 220 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 19

DISPOSITION: CONVICTIONS AND SENTENCES AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the convictions for burglary and first degree murder and the consequent death sentence adjudged against him by the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona).


OVERVIEW: A jury found that defendant robbed two shopkeepers and killed them with a Bowie knife. The jury therefore convicted defendant of burglary and first degree premeditated murder, and condemned him to die. Defendant appealed. The court held that: (1) Ariz. R. Crim. Post-conviction relief denied at, Petition granted by State v. Laird, 2016 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1230 (Oct. 4, 2016)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Laird v. Ryan, 2017 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 63822 (D. Ariz., Apr. 26, 2017)

DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of Arizona in Maricopa County of first degree murder, one count of dangerous kidnapping, one count of dangerous burglary, one count of theft, four counts of forgery, and one count of robbery. He was sentenced to death for the murder and to various prison terms for the remaining convictions. Appeal to the court was automatic.


OVERVIEW: Defendant broke into the victim's home, waited for her to return, and murdered her in an attempt to steal her truck. He dumped her body in the woods and covered it with vegetation. Defendant, while a minor, understood the nature of his actions and his crime did not show immaturity or impulsivity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions of murder, kidnapping, burglary, theft, forgery, and robbery, as well as his death sentence and prison terms.

State v. Towery


Supreme Court No. CR-92-0493-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


186 Ariz. 168; 920 P.2d 290; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 65; 220 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



June 27, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied February 18, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Maricopa County No. CR 91-92648. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Cheryl K. Hendrix, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with six counts: first degree murder, armed robbery, first degree burglary, kidnapping, theft, and attempted theft. The Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona), convicted defendant of the charged offenses and sentenced him to death for the murder conviction and concurrent terms for the other counts. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that his right to confrontation was violated, that testimony about his accomplice's religious beliefs was not offered to impeach his credibility but to show that his beliefs aided the accomplice to engage in criminal conduct and to commit the murder, that his equal protection and due process rights were violated, that the prosecutor used the same evidence to convict defendant of two separate crimes, and that the trial judge's finding that he committed the murder in an especially cruel, heinous, or depraved manner was erroneous. The court held that: (1) the limitation imposed on the cross-examination was proper because it protected the attorney-client privilege; (2) that the trial court did not err when it precluded evidence of the accomplice's religious beliefs because there was no probative value apart from the attack on the witness's credibility; (3) that defendant's equal protection rights were not violated when he was denied a free transcript; (4) that the prosecutor's misconduct did not affect the verdict; and (5) that there were sufficient aggravating factors that defendant committed the murder in a cruel manner.


OUTCOME: Defendant's first degree murder conviction and the death sentence were affirmed.

State v. Lee


Supreme Court Nos. CR-93-0111-AP and CR-93-0323-T/AP [Consolidated]


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


185 Ariz. 549; 917 P.2d 692; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 58; 217 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



May 21, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Claim dismissed by, Motion denied by Lee v. Schriro, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 71382 (D. Ariz., Sept. 25, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] La Paz County Superior Court. No. CR-92-01-13. Appeal from the Superior Court of La Paz County. The Honorable Michael Irwin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTIONS AND DEATH SENTENCE AFFIRMED; OTHER SENTENCES MODIFIED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his murder conviction and death sentence in the La Paz County Superior Court (Arizona). Defendant argued that the prosecutor impermissibly vouched for state's witnesses during closing, that the trial court improperly denied his motion to change venue due to pretrial publicity, that the trial court improperly considered aggravating circumstances in sentencing defendant, and that mitigating factors were not considered.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. Defendant sought review of the conviction and sentence, arguing that the prosecutor improperly vouched for prosecutorial witnesses during closing argument, that the trial court erred in refusing to grant defendant a change in venue due to pretrial publicity, that the prosecutor filed a notice to seek the death penalty after the expiration of the legal time limit set out in Ariz. R. Crim. P. 15(g)(1), that the trial court, in sentencing defendant, improperly considered aggravating factors, and that the trial court failed to consider mitigating factors. The court affirmed, holding that it was proper for the trial court to consider defendant's prior conviction for robbery, pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(2), because such an offense involved the use or threat of violence. The court also held that the trial court properly considered as an aggravating circumstance the fact that the murder was committed for pecuniary gain. The state proved that the receipt of money was a cause and motivation for the murder. Defendant did not meet his burden of proving that pretrial publicity required a change of venue.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's murder conviction and death sentence, holding that the trial court properly determined that defendant's murder of the victim for the expectation of pecuniary gain was properly considered an aggravating circumstance. Defendant further did not meet his burden of proving presumptive prejudice resulting from pretrial publicity, and thus the trial court properly overruled defendant's motion for change of venue.

State v. Mata


Supreme Court No. CR-77-4104-AP/PC [4104]


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


185 Ariz. 319; 916 P.2d 1035; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 50; 216 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 48



May 9, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected May 15, 1996. Second Correction July 31, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County No. 97310.



DISPOSITION: TEMPORARY STAY QUASHED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. After the court (Arizona) issued a warrant scheduling his execution, appellant moved the court to stay the execution. The court issued a temporary stay of the warrant to consider only two of the legal questions presented.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. The court ordered a temporary stay to consider two issues. The court quashed the stay and held that appellant did not need to be resentenced pursuant to subsequent caselaw, and his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel was precluded. Federal or state constitutional law did not require resentencing despite the use of the statutory aggravating circumstance of "especially heinous, cruel or depraved" to death-qualify him. The aggravating circumstance was adequately narrowed at the time of appellant's sentence; the subsequent caselaw merely finely combed for narrowing language and provided no construction of the aggravating circumstance. Appellant was precluded from claiming ineffective assistance of counsel for not presenting mitigating evidence because (1) he had presented this "newly discovered evidence" before and was precluded from presenting it again, and (2) to the extent that he had not raised ineffective assistance claims or presented evidence to which he had access in previous post-conviction proceedings, he had knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily waived the right to do so.


OUTCOME: The court quashed the temporary stay of execution and denied appellant's motion to stay.

State v. Jones


Supreme Court CR-93-0541-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


185 Ariz. 471; 917 P.2d 200; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 52; 216 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 36



May 7, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Habeas corpus proceeding at, Motion granted by, Motion granted by, in part, Motion denied by, in part Jones v. Schriro, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 3454 (D. Ariz., Jan. 27, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Mohave County Superior Court CR-14141. Appeal from the Superior Court of Mohave County. The Honorable James E. Chavez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Mohave County, Arizona, which convicted of two counts of premeditated first degree murder and one count of attempted premeditated first degree murder. Defendant was sentenced to two consecutive death sentences for the murders and to a consecutive sentence of life imprisonment for the attempted murder. Defendant's convictions and sentences were automatically appealed.


OVERVIEW: Regarding the first murder, the court found the following aggravating circumstances: (1) defendant committed the murder for pecuniary gain; (2) the murder was especially cruel, heinous, or depraved; and (3) defendant was convicted of another homicide that he committed during the commission of the instant offense. Regarding the second murder, the court found the following aggravating circumstances: (1) defendant committed the murder for pecuniary gain; (2) the murder was especially cruel, heinous, or depraved; (3) defendant was convicted of another homicide that he committed during the commission of the instant offense; and (4) the victim was under 15 years of age. Regarding both murders, the court gave mitigating weight to defendant's history of substance abuse, intoxication at the time of the offenses, and head injuries, but found that the mitigating circumstances were insufficiently substantial to call for leniency. The court held that defendant waived several issues alleged as error by failing to raise them in the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences.

 

44 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Zamora v. Reinstein


Supreme Court CV-95-0335-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


185 Ariz. 272; 915 P.2d 1227; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 54; 216 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 23



May 7, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals 1 CA-SA 94-0324. Maricopa County Superior Court CR 94-04807. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. Honorable Ronald S. Reinstein, Judge. Court of Appeals, Division One, 183 Ariz. 470, 904 P.2d 1294 (1995).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County AFFIRMED. Court of Appeals, Division One, VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution challenged a finding by the Court of Appeals (Arizona), which reversed the decision of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), in finding that under Arizona's sentence enhancement statute, Ariz. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 13-614(U)(1)(a), the trial court could only enhance a criminal sentence if it found that defendant's prior convictions both mandated a prison term and met one of the other qualifications of types of crimes.


OVERVIEW: After defendant was convicted, the prosecution sought to enhance his sentence with prior convictions pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 13-604(U)(1)(a). The trial court allowed the enhancement, finding that the language of the statute allowed for enhancement if the defendant had been convicted of one of the following: a prior felony whose conviction mandated a prison term, a crime that involved physical injury, a crime that involved the use of a deadly or dangerous weapon, the illegal control of a criminal enterprise, or a crime against children as defined by statute. The appeals court disagreed with the trial court's interpretation of the statute, finding that in order to enhance, the state had to prove that the conviction mandated a prison term and one of the other factors enumerated by statute. The prosecution appealed. The court agreed with the trial court, holding that based upon legislative history, the trial court could have enhanced defendant's sentence pursuant to § 13-604(U)(1)(a) if it found that any of the elements were present.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the appellate court and remanded the cause to the trial court.

 

45 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Miles


No. CR-93-0333-AP.


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


186 Ariz. 10; 918 P.2d 1028; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 136; 216 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



May 3, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at State v. Jackson, 186 Ariz. 20, 918 P.2d 1038, 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 137 (Ariz., 1996)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Miles v. Ryan, 2012 U.S. App. LEXIS 18126 (9th Cir. Ariz., Aug. 27, 2012)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's appeal was automatic for his convictions for first degree murder, dangerous kidnapping, and dangerous armed robbery. Defendant was sentenced to death for the murder and sentenced to prison terms for the non-capital convictions. Defendant contended, among other things, that the Arizona trial court erred in admitting his statements to police detectives and in instructing the jury on felony murder.


OVERVIEW: The victim was stopped, in her car, at an intersection when defendant's cohort pulled out a gun and ordered the victim to scoot over. Defendant and another cohort got in the vehicle. One cohort had previously told defendant that the cohort was going to get somebody's car, take them off in the middle of the desert, and shoot them, which was exactly what happened. As defendant watched from the car, the victim pleaded for her life, but was shot nonetheless. The court reviewed the record for fundamental error and, finding none, affirmed the convictions and sentences. Defendant was caught after he was found driving the victim's car. In his possession were the victim's ring, earrings, bank card, and credit card. The court found that the murder was committed for pecuniary gain and that the murder was especially cruel, given that the victim experienced mental anguish as to the uncertainty of her ultimate fate. However, the state did not prove beyond a reasonable doubt that the murder, at least as to defendant, was committed in an especially heinous or depraved manner, but the death penalty was, nonetheless, not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder, one count of dangerous kidnapping, and one count of dangerous armed robbery. The court also affirmed the sentence to death for the murder and the prison terms for the non-capital convictions.

State v. Jackson


No. CR-94-0045-AP.


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


186 Ariz. 20; 918 P.2d 1038; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 137; 216 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 9



May 3, 1996, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied December 2, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 7290.
Writ of certiorari denied Jackson v. Arizona, 519 U.S. 1015, 117 S. Ct. 527, 136 L. Ed. 2d 413, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 7290 (1996)
Decision reached on appeal by State v. Jackson, 2006 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 536 (2006)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Jackson v. Ryan, 2009 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 112973 (D. Ariz., Nov. 17, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Miles, 186 Ariz. 10, 918 P.2d 1028, 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 136 (Ariz., 1996)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from an Arizona trial court, which convicted him of first degree murder, one count of dangerous kidnapping, and one count of dangerous armed robbery and sentenced him to death for the murder and to prison terms for the non-capital convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a 16-year-old, and two accomplices carjacked the victim. Defendant drove the victim to a desert area where he shot her after she repeatedly pleaded for her life. The trial court convicted and sentenced defendant to death. The court affirmed. The trial court did not abuse its discretion when it allowed the State to file its notice of intent to seek the death penalty, as required by Ariz. R. Crim. P. 15.1(g)(1), one day late because defendant was aware of the State's intent to seek the death penalty and was not prejudiced by the late filing. It was constitutional to sentence a 16-year-old individual to death. Defendant's motion for change of venue was properly denied because the pretrial publicity was not pervasive or outrageous. The State proved the aggravating circumstances that the crime was committed in a cruel, heinous, or depraved manner under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(6). Although defendant's age was a mitigating factor under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(G)(5), he was not entitled to leniency because he was intelligent and mature, was a major participant in the crime, acted with obvious deliberateness, and had a history of assaultive behavior.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and his death sentence.

STATE v. ROSCOE


Supreme Court No. CR-95-0023-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


185 Ariz. 68; 912 P.2d 1297; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 13; 211 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 11



February 29, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals No. 2 CA-CR 92-0987. Pima County Nos. CR-34685 and CR-37461 [Consolidated]. Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County. The Honorable Alice Truman, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, 182 Ariz. 332, 897 P.2d 634 (App. 1994).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County AFFIRMED. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, VACATED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases arising from two separate arrests, the trial court convicted defendant of two counts of aggravated assault on a peace officer, one count of disorderly conduct, and one count of resisting arrest. After the Court of Appeals (Arizona) affirmed, defendant filed a petition for review with the court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's petition for review contended that the appellate court erred by declaring both Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4433(F) and Ariz. R. Crim. P. 39(b)(11) to be unconstitutional restrictions on the Victims' Bill of Rights, Ariz. Const. art II, § 2.1. The issue presented was whether the provisions of § 13-4433(F) and Rule 39(b)(11), which denied on-duty peace officers status as "victims," unconstitutional because they conflicted with the definition of "victim" set forth in Ariz. Const. art. II, § 2.1 (C). The court found that the constitutional provision in the case was not ambiguous. The authors of the Victims' Bill of Rights clearly defined victim and just as clearly defined two exceptions to victim status in Ariz. Const. art. II, § 2.1 (C). The court further found that defendant presented no authority, nor was the court aware of any, which supported the proposition that a court was empowered to depart from the unambiguous language of a constitutional provision. The court concluded that the definition of victim in the Victims' Bill of Rights did not infringe on defendant's right to due process by denying him access to evidence favorable to an accused.


OUTCOME: To the extent that they conflicted with the definition of the term "victim" as provided in the Victims' Bill of Rights, the statute and the rule were unconstitutional. The court agreed with the appellate court's resolution of the issue and affirmed the convictions and sentences of the trial court.

State v. Roscoe


Supreme Court No. CR-93-0278-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


184 Ariz. 484; 910 P.2d 635; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 12; 209 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3

DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona), which convicted him of child molestation, kidnapping, and murder, and which sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Despite defendant's alibi defense, the jury found defendant guilty of child molestation, kidnapping and murder. The court found that (1) other bad acts evidence, of two other incidents with female children, was admissible to show modus operandi for one very similar incident, and to show deviant propensities for the other incident as its probative value was not substantially outweighed by unfair prejudice, (2) photographs of the victim were admissible as they were relevant to disputed issues and were not particularly gruesome, (3) the trial court properly excluded expert testimony on eyewitnesses where the case against defendant did not depend on an eyewitness, (4) the prosecutor's comment about evidence not admitted at trial was cured by an instruction, (5) defendant was not denied a unanimous verdict where the jurors did not agree on a single murder theory, (6) defendant's counsel could exclude certain mitigating factors, (7) defendant was eligible for the death penalty as the murder was cruel, and (8) the trial court did not fail to consider all mitigating factors as defendant's lack of felony convictions was contradicted by a violent misdemeanor.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant and sentencing him to death.

State v. Spears


Supreme Court CR-93-0139-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


184 Ariz. 277; 908 P.2d 1062; 1996 Ariz. LEXIS 1; 207 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



January 4, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court CR-92-90457. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable Cheryl K. Hendrix, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) convicted defendant of theft and premeditated first degree murder and sentenced him to four years' imprisonment for the theft and to death for the murder. Defendant appealed a variety of issues, including the trial court's failure to suppress evidence, to grant a new trial based on juror misconduct, the sufficiency of the evidence, and the constitutionality of the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: The court found no error in the denial of suppression of evidence or a new trial and that the evidence was sufficient to support the convictions. In reweighing the aggravating and mitigating factors considered in imposing the death penalty, the court found slightly more mitigating factors than the trial court but not sufficient to warrant leniency. There was ample evidence supporting the finding of probable cause to arrest defendant and the validity of the search warrant. Defendant's statements were properly admitted because the conditions of incarceration of which defendant complained were not sufficient to overcome his free will. Alleged juror misconduct was inconsequential in the absence of a showing of actual prejudice. In prior opinions, the court rejected all of defendant's arguments that the death penalty was unconstitutional. The restitution order was proper under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-603(C). The trial court's finding of an aggravating factor, that the murder was for pecuniary gain, was supported by the evidence. The trial court properly refused to find the mitigating circumstance of residual doubt because the verdict was supported by very strong evidence.


OUTCOME: The defendant's convictions and sentences were affirmed.

City of Phoenix v. Yarnell


Supreme Court No. CV-94-0416-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


184 Ariz. 310; 909 P.2d 377; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 117; 205 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 44



December 14, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 8, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeals 1 CA-SA 93-0327. Maricopa County No. CV 92-01578. Special Action from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Michael A. Yarnell, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 180 Ariz. 472, 885 P.2d 160 (App. 1994).



DISPOSITION: Special Action from the Superior Court in Maricopa County, REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, Vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff arrestee sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals, Division One (Arizona), that ordered the trial court to enter summary judgment for defendants, detectives, city, and county, on plaintiff's claims asserted under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Defendants had petitioned for relief by special action from an order of respondent superior court judge that denied their motions for summary judgment in plaintiff's tort action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff arrestee, who was incarcerated for nine months in spite of clear evidence of another's guilt, brought a tort action against defendants detectives, city, and county. Defendants sought summary judgment on plaintiff's 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claims. On review from the court of appeals, the court affirmed the order granting judgment in favor of defendants, city and detectives. The court held that defendant detectives, who had urged the prosecutor to drop the charges, were not liable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 for any injury resulting from the prosecution. Because the detectives did not violate any of respondent's constitutional rights, their employer, the city, was not liable to plaintiff. Summary judgment in defendant county's favor was reversed. The court held that the court of appeals erred in entering summary judgment on issues not raised by the movant in the trial court and on which the parties had not had an opportunity to marshal and present evidence. The case was remanded to provide plaintiff with an opportunity to investigate whether the decision to continue his prosecution and to withhold exculpatory material was made by a county official with final policymaking authority.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order in plaintiff arrestee's tort action granting judgment in favor of defendants, city and detectives on plaintiff's claims. Because the record reflected genuine issues of material fact concerning defendant county' potential liability, the court vacated the court of appeals' opinion and its order directing entry of summary judgment in favor of defendant county.

State v. Gulbrandson


Supreme Court CR-93-0085-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


184 Ariz. 46; 906 P.2d 579; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 105; 202 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 46



November 2, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Gulbrandson v. Arizona, 518 U.S. 1022, 116 S. Ct. 2558, 135 L. Ed. 2d 1076, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 4134 (1996)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Certificate of appealability denied Gulbrandson v. Schriro, 2007 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 25324 (D. Ariz., Mar. 31, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County Superior Court. CR-91-90974. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable David L. Grounds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant received an automatic appeal from the order of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which convicted him of premeditated first degree murder and theft and sentenced him to death. The trial court found the aggravating circumstance of "especially heinous, cruel or depraved" murder under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(F)(6) resulting from (1) relishing of the murder, (2) gratuitous violence, and (3) the helplessness of the victim.


OVERVIEW: Defendant killed his former lover after an earlier assault and threats. The victim was cut or stabbed 34 times, was tied, beaten, and probably choked. On appeal, the court held that while an initial check-welfare sweep of the defendant's apartment was unlawful, a subsequent search under a warrant was lawful under the Fourth Amendment and the Arizona privacy provision, Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 8, under the independent source doctrine. The court found evidence of the prior assault was admissible under Ariz. R. Evid. 404(b) because its probative value as to motive outweighed any prejudice. The court independently reviewed aggravating and mitigating circumstances in the entire record to determine whether the death penalty was proper. The court held that evidence that defendant took the victim's car and went gambling in another state after the murder did not support a finding that he relished the murder. However, the court found there was ample evidence to support findings of helplessness and gratuitous violence, and the two together supported a finding of "especially heinous, cruel or depraved" murder. The court agreed that the mitigation evidence was insufficient to justify leniency.


OUTCOME: The court found that the convictions of murder and theft were proper. The court affirmed the trial court's finding of an aggravating circumstance and independently reviewed all the circumstances, reweighed the aggravating circumstance and the mitigating circumstances, and affirmed defendant's death sentence.

Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Towery, 204 Ariz. 386, 64 P.3d 828, 2003 Ariz. LEXIS 16 (Ariz., 2003)

Aitken v. Industrial Comm'n


Supreme Court No. CV-92-0257-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


183 Ariz. 387; 904 P.2d 456; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 98; 201 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 18



October 17, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied May 20, 1996, Reported at: 1996 U.S. LEXIS 3278.


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of Appeals No. 2 CA-IC 91-0042. Industrial Commission No. 374-78-9448. Carrier No. A065140. Appeal from the Industrial Commission. Administrative Law Judge Jerry C. Schmidt. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two. 173 Ariz. 300, 842, P.2d 1313 (Ct. App. 1992).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Industrial Commission AWARD SET ASIDE. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two VACATED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner injured employee appealed a decision of the Arizona Industrial Commission that was affirmed by the court of appeals. The decision was in favor of respondent carrier and held that a lien credit claimed by respondent could not be reduced merely because the insured employer shared responsibility for petitioner's injuries.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner injured employee brought a third party suit alleging a construction company was partially responsible for her injury. The company responded by alleging contributory negligence and naming respondent employer as a nonparty at fault. The jury apportioned 65 percent of the liability to the construction company, 25 percent to respondent employer, and 10 percent to petitioner and, after reducing the award by petitioner's and respondent employer's percentages of fault, entered judgment against the construction company. Petitioner reimbursed respondent insurer for benefits previously collected and respondent insurer thereafter claimed a lien against future compensation in the amount of the judgment remaining after attorney fees and costs. Petitioner asserted that both the lien and the credit were excessive. The administrative law judge held that the lien could not be reduced and the court of appeals affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that respondent carrier could assert a lien only to the extent that the compensation benefits paid exceeded the employer's proportionate share of the damages fixed by verdict. The court set aside the award and remanded the case.


OUTCOME: The court set aside the award of damages, vacated the opinion of the court of appeals, and remanded the case. The court held that respondent carrier could assert a lien only to the extent that the compensation benefits paid exceeded the employer's proportionate share of the damages fixed by verdict.

Jimenez v. Sears, Roebuck & Co.


Supreme Court No. CV-94-0273-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


183 Ariz. 399; 904 P.2d 861; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 120; 201 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 22; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P14,382



October 17, 1995, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals No. 2 CA-CV 93-0243. Pima County No. 274330. Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County. The Honorable John F. Kelly, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two. 180 Ariz. 432, 885 P.2d 120 (App. 1994).



DISPOSITION: Appeal from the Superior Court reversed: Court of Appeals, vacated in part


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, husband and wife, appealed from the order of the Court of Appeals (Arizona) that reversed the trial court's order which refused to grant defendant corporation's request that the jury be instructed on comparative fault and ordered a new trial in plaintiffs' action against defendant which alleged that defendant sold a defective and unreasonably dangerous product that caused serious injury to plaintiff husband.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, a husband and wife, filed an action against defendant corporation, alleging that it sold them a defective and unreasonably dangerous power tool after a disc in a hand-held electric grinder shattered and injured plaintiff husband. Defendant asserted the product misuse defense and requested a jury charge on comparative fault. The trial court refused to instruct the jury on comparative negligence, and damages were awarded to plaintiffs. The court of appeals reversed and ordered a new trial on the ground that it was error not to give a jury charge on comparative fault. The court vacated parts of the court of appeals' opinion and remanded, holding that the Uniform Contribution Among Tortfeasors Act, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-2505, did not constitute a limitation on damages. The court affirmed the order which held that the trial court erred in its refusal to give a jury charge on comparative fault because § 12-2505 provided for the defense of product misuse in a strict liability case. The court held that the imposition of the product misuse defense to a comparative fault analysis did not violate Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 31 or the limitation clause of Ariz. Const. art. 18, § 6.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the court of appeals' opinion that a statute which provided for a product misuse defense in strict liability cases constituted a limitation on damages. The court affirmed the order which held that the trial court erred in refusing to instruct a jury on comparative fault in a strict liability action against defendant manufacturer because there was statutory support for imposing a product misuse defense in a strict liability case.

STATE v. WILLIAMS


Supreme Court No. CR-93-0138-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


183 Ariz. 368; 904 P.2d 437; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 96; 200 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 11



September 26, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 11, 1995.
Motions ruled upon by, Habeas corpus proceeding at Williams v. Schriro, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 12424 (D. Ariz., Mar. 20, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Pinal County Nos. CR-15716 and CR-16291 (Consolidated). Appeal from the Superior Court of Pinal County. The Honorable Robert R. Bean, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Pinal County, Arizona, found defendant guilty of first degree murder, attempted first degree murder, and armed robbery. He was sentenced to death. This was defendant's automatic, direct appeal pursuant to Ariz. R. Crim. P. 31.2(b) and Ariz. Rev. Stat. Ann. §§ 13-4031, 13-4035 (1989).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murdering his former girlfriend. Five weeks later he robbed a convenience store and shot the cashier, whom he knew. She survived and testified against him. On appeal, the court considered defendant's trial issues. The trial court did not err by consolidating the two cases because they were properly consolidated as otherwise connected together in their commission within the meaning of Ariz. R. Evid. 13.3(a)(2). The trial court admitted prior bad acts evidence within Ariz. R. Evid. 404(b) (proper purpose), Ariz. R. Evid. 402 (relevance), Ariz. R. Evid. 403 (unfair prejudice substantially outweighs the probative value), and Ariz. R. Evid. 105's (provision for an appropriate limiting instruction). A mistrial should not have been granted after a witness testified concerning defendant's prior bad acts because Ariz. R. Crim. P. 15 imposes no disclosure requirement. Preindictment delay did not deny defendant his right to a speedy trial or due process because his Sixth Amendment right to a speedy trial did not attach until he was indicted. Ariz. R. Evid. 615(3) was not violated when the trial court allowed the state two investigative witnesses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court regarding defendant's convictions and sentences. The court considered all the issues that defendant raised and found that his convictions were proper. The court conducted an independent review of defendant's sentence of death and agreed with the trial court that the death penalty was appropriate. The court searched the record and found no fundamental error.

State v. Bolton


Supreme Court No. CR-93-0086-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


182 Ariz. 290; 896 P.2d 830; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 49; 192 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 49



June 13, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected September 19, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County No. CR-33385. Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County. The Honorable William L. Scholl, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Superior Court of Pima County (Arizona), which convicted and sentenced him for kidnapping, burglary, and first degree felony murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised several contentions regarding his trial and sentencing. The court held that 1) the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for change of venue because the pretrial publicity did not have such an impact on the community that prejudice could be presumed and there was no evidence that such publicity affected the jury's objectivity or its ability to render a fair verdict, 2) the state properly exercised its peremptory challenges to remove persons with reservations about the death penalty, 3) the admission of defendant's prior convictions on the issue of credibility was harmless error because any potential prejudice of such admission was redundant to the prejudice caused by defendant's own testimony that he was a thief and had committed a murder other than the one charged, 4) a gruesome photograph of the victim was properly admitted because it was relevant and the probative value outweighed the danger of prejudice, and 5) the trial court properly found that the murder was committed in an especially cruel manner because there was evidence that the victim was conscious during the attack and suffered both physical pain and mental anguish before she died.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Business Realty v. Maricopa County


Supreme Court No. CV-93-0374-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


181 Ariz. 551; 892 P.2d 1340; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 28; 187 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 5



April 4, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals No. 1 CA-TX 92-0005. Arizona Tax Court No. TX 90-00887. Appeal from the Arizona Tax Court. The Honorable Kenneth W. Reeves, III, Commissioner. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 178 Ariz. 29, 870 P.2d 1125 (Ct. App. 1993). VACATED.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Taxpayer protested county's valuation of its shopping center, based on the cost approach to valuation, and elected to have the property valued in accordance with the straight line building residual (SLBR) method of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 42-147(B). Using the SLBR method, county reduced the value of the property. On appeal, the tax court granted a further reduction the Court of Appeals (Arizona) affirmed. The appeal followed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court examined the language and legislative history of § 42-147 and explained that the statute established a property valuation system designed to estimate fair market value. The court further explained that the statute allowed a reviewing body to consider all facts and techniques recognized by the appraisal profession in determining fair market value. The court found that if the SLBR technique provided a figure that fell within the range of reasonable market values, it should be upheld on final review. The court held, however, that if the value estimate from use of the SLBR income method produced a figure not reasonably within the range of market value, then the final reviewing body could use all other relevant information and standard techniques of valuation to determine fair market value. § 42-147(D). The court vacated the decision of the intermediate appellate court, reversed the decision of the tax court, and remanded the case to the tax court. The court ordered the tax court to follow the statutory procedure for estimating value in order to determine the property's market value.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the intermediate appellate court, reversed the judgment of the tax court, and remanded the case to the tax court for a determination of the market value of the property.

State v. Willoughby


Supreme Court No. CR-92-0439-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


181 Ariz. 530; 892 P.2d 1319; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 23; 186 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 45



March 23, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Willoughby v. Arizona, 516 U.S. 1054, 116 S. Ct. 725, 133 L. Ed. 2d 677, 1996 U.S. LEXIS 187 (1996)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Willoughby v. Ryan, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 143184 (D. Ariz., Sept. 30, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County No. CR 91-10184. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable Joseph D. Howe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In the Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona), a jury found defendant guilty of first-degree murder and conspiracy to commit murder. Defendant was subsequently sentenced to death, and this appeal was automatic.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and his wife, who lived in Arizona, went on a trip to Mexico. The wife was found dead in their rented condominium, and defendant was later arrested, tried, and convicted of first-degree murder. The court held that 1) Arizona had jurisdiction to prosecute defendant under Ariz. Rev. Stat. 13-108(A)(1), because the state proved that an element of the crime, premeditation, occurred in the state, 2) while the trial judge improperly applied the preponderance of the evidence standard to determine whether the state had the authority to prosecute, such error was harmless because the evidence established that part of the crime took place in the state, 3) the statute conferred jurisdiction over acts that took place in a foreign country, because there were no limiting words in the statute, which was not unconstitutional, 4) there was no conflict between Arizona law and international law because Mexico cooperated in defendant's prosecution, and 5) leniency was not appropriate based on the strength of the aggravating circumstance that defendant killed his wife for financial gain.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's first-degree murder conviction and his death sentence.

State v. Barreras


Supreme Court No. CR-92-0343-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


181 Ariz. 516; 892 P.2d 852; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 26; 186 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 40



March 23, 1995, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County No. CR 91-00182. Appeal from the Superior Court in Maricopa County. The Honorable B. Michael Dann, Judge.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTION AFFIRMED, SENTENCE REDUCED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant entered a plea of no contest to one count of first-degree murder and one count of sexual assault. The Superior Court in Maricopa County (Arizona) sentenced defendant to death and a consecutive 21-year prison term. Defendant sought review of his sentencing hearing and the death sentence imposed by the trial court.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the trial court did not commit any fundamental errors. The trial court complied with Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4013 by providing defendant access to competent expert assistance and properly denied defendant's motion for continuance. Accordingly, the court affirmed the convictions. The death sentence, however, was reduced. The trial court found that defendant committed the murder in an especially heinous and depraved manner and that the mitigating evidence was not substantial enough to require leniency. While the court acknowledged the problems inherent in leaving the ultimate decision of life or death to human judgment, the court knew of no other way to accomplish the task and upheld the weighing of aggravating and mitigating circumstances under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(E). The court found that the murder was senseless beyond a reasonable doubt, but that the record did not support a finding beyond a reasonable doubt that witness elimination motivated defendant. Although the question was close, the court concluded that the facts did not support a finding beyond a reasonable doubt that the murder was especially heinous or depraved.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions, but reduced the death sentence to a term of life imprisonment without possibility of release for 25 years consecutive to all other sentences.

Wilderness World v. Department of Revenue


Supreme Court CV-94-0122-PR, CV-94-0501-PR (Consolidated)


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


182 Ariz. 196; 895 P.2d 108; 1995 Ariz. LEXIS 37; 188 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 23



April, 18, 1995, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Amended June 9, 1995.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals 1 CA-TX 92-0016. Arizona Tax Court TX-92-00109. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Tax Court. The Honorable William J. Schafer, III, Judge. Arizona Court of Appeals, Division One 180 Ariz. 155, 159, 882 P.2d 1281, 1285 (App. 1993, 1994). AFFIRMED IN PART, REVERSED IN PART.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee Arizona Department of Revenue sought review of a decision by the Arizona Court of Appeals, Division One, which reversed a tax court judgment that upheld the imposition of an additional transaction privilege tax, together with interest and penalties, against appellant, an oar-powered river rafting service corporation that operated in Grand Canyon National Park.


OVERVIEW: The department levied an additional transaction privilege tax against the corporation under former Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 42-1314(A)(1). The corporation exhausted its administrative appeals, and filed a complaint in the tax court, which granted the department's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the tax court judgment was reversed, which the department appealed. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the appellate court's decision and held that: (1) the corporation did not charge an admission fee so § 42-1314(A)(1) was inapplicable; (2) the doctrine of ejusdem generis was applicable; (3) river rafting trips were not of the same kind or nature of the activities that were taxable under § 42-1310.14(A)(1); (4) river rafting trips were not an "amusement" under § 42-1314(A)(1); (5) the decision applied retroactively; (6) a prior appellate court opinion was disapproved; (7) stare decisis was inapplicable because a lower appellate court decision was not binding on the state's supreme court; (8) Ariz. Const. art. IX, § 3 was inapplicable; and (9) the appellate court abused its discretion by denying attorneys' fees to the corporation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and denied in part the appellate court's reversal of a tax court judgment that upheld the imposition of an additional transaction privilege tax on the corporation.

Smith v. Myers


Supreme Court No. CV-92-0069-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


181 Ariz. 11; 887 P.2d 541; 1994 Ariz. LEXIS 137; 181 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



December 29, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals No. 1 CA-SA 91-210. Maricopa County No. CV 90-18731. Petition for Special Action from the Maricopa County Superior Court, The Honorable Robert D. Myers, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 171 Ariz. 511, 831 P.2d 1279 (1991), Vacated.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff husband and wife filed a wrongful death action alleging medical malpractice against petitioners, surviving spouse of doctor and others, who elected to have the case tried under Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 12-581 to -594, providing for periodic payment of future economic losses. Plaintiffs raised constitutional challenges, which were sustained by respondent trial judge. The Court of Appeals, Division One (Arizona) granted relief to petitioners.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff husband and wife filed a wrongful death action alleging medical malpractice against petitioners, surviving spouse of doctor and others, who elected to have the case tried under Arizona's periodic payment statutes, Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 12-581 to -594, which provided for the periodic payment of future economic losses. Plaintiffs' objection to employing the method was overruled, but plaintiffs' constitutional challenges were sustained. The court of appeals granted relief to defendants and the court vacated the opinion, holding that the periodic payment statutes violated Ariz. Const. art. II, § 31 by limiting medical malpractice victims to a significantly less valuable remedy than would otherwise be available. The court stated that § 31 represented a fundamental guaranty intended to have broad sweep to protect all injured persons, and that it could not ignore such an extraordinary protection. The court stated that any award for future damages could only be a reasonable guess, and when any estimate of future loss proved to be incorrect, common wisdom and sound public policy dictated that the error should inure to the benefit of the tort victim rather than the tortfeasor.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the appellate court's opinion and held that the periodic payment statutes were effectively a damage limitation that violated the Arizona Constitution by limiting medical malpractice victims to a significantly less valuable remedy than would otherwise be available. The court reasoned that a promise of future installment payments was not the equivalent of a lump sum award.

State v. Ross


Supreme Court No. CR-91-0133-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


180 Ariz. 598; 886 P.2d 1354; 1994 Ariz. LEXIS 138; 180 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



December 22, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County No. CR-30722. Appeal from the Superior Court of Arizona in Pima County. The Honorable Michael D. Alfred, Judge Pro Tem.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant defendant was convicted of premeditated first degree murder and armed robbery. He appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Arizona in Pima County sentencing him to death and life imprisonment, asserting that the court erred in denying his motions to suppress his confession, that he was denied his right to effective counsel and that the court improperly found certain aggravating factors and overlooked mitigating ones.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed his convictions for premeditated first degree murder and armed robbery and death sentence, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4031. The court affirmed the trial court's convictions and sentence, finding that the totality of circumstances surrounding defendant's confession suggested that it had been voluntary, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-3988(B). The court also found that defendant's assertion that he received ineffective assistance of counsel could not be raised on postconviction relief proceedings, Ariz. R. Crim. P. 32. Defendant contended that the trial court improperly considered aggravating circumstances in assigning capital punishment, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(C), (F), and the court agreed, finding that he had murdered for pecuniary gain but had not done so in a heinous and depraved fashion, § 13-703(F)(5), (6). In reaching this decision, the court noted that the killing was senseless but was not done to eliminate a witness. Finally, the court rejected the allegedly mitigating circumstances raised by defendant, noting that a difficult family background was not a mitigating circumstance unless it had an effect on a defendant's behavior beyond his control.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

STATE v. RICHMOND


Supreme Court No. CR-80-2914-AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


180 Ariz. 573; 886 P.2d 1329; 1994 Ariz. LEXIS 131; 179 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 57



December 15, 1994, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pima County No. A-24252. On Remand from the United States Supreme Court Richmond v. Lewis,     U.S.    , 113 S. Ct. 528 (1992).



DISPOSITION: SENTENCE REDUCED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant death row inmate had exhausted all appeal routes available to him. He had been resentenced again to death, and thereafter filed a habeas corpus petition, which the United States Supreme Court granted unless appellee state corrected the constitutional error or reduced the sentence. The trial court, the Pima County Superior Court (Arizona) referred the case to the court for further proceedings or a clarification of the record.


OVERVIEW: The death row inmate was on death row for over 20 years when the case was referred to the court from the trial court. The United States Supreme Court had granted his petition for habeas corpus unless the constitutional errors in the sentencing were corrected or a reduced sentence imposed on him, and remanded to the trial court. The trial judge sua sponte referred the case to the court. The court determined that the more efficient course was for it to reweigh the aggravating and mitigating factors to correct the constitutional error, rather than remand for a full sentencing hearing, even though ordinarily the trial court should conduct the reweighing. It had the authority to reweigh because the state conceded that a sentence reduction was preferable to a remand. It held that the aggravating factors of a prior felony and the commission of the crime in an especially heinous, cruel or depraved manner were improper. It held that the mitigation was more than de minimus. There was significant evidence of his changed character. It noted that under the sentencing requirements it was obligated to consider mental disorders and at the time of the murder he was under the influence of drugs.


OUTCOME: The court determined that the better solution concerning the inmate on death row for 20 years was to reweigh the aggravating circumstances and the mitigating circumstances surrounding his conviction on murder charges, rather than remand for further resentencing procedures. The court reduced the death sentence to life imprisonment.

Wohlstrom v. Buchanan


Supreme Court No. CV-92-0316-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


180 Ariz. 389; 884 P.2d 687; 1994 Ariz. LEXIS 123; 177 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 29



November 3, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals. No. 2 CA-SA 92-0075. Pima County. No. 283609. Petition for Review from the Superior Court of Pima County. The Honorable Robert B. Buchanan



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state, as real party in interest, initiated forfeiture proceedings pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 13-4305 to 13-4315 for monies seized under warrant from petitioner claimant's luggage. The claimant filed a timely claim that was struck by the Superior Court of Pima County (Arizona) for failure to comply with Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4311(E). The claimant filed a petition for special action, alleging that § 13-4311E violated U.S. Const. amend. V.


OVERVIEW: In forfeiture proceedings, the court agreed with the claimant's contention that striking his claim for failure to comply with § 13-4311E violated U.S. Const. amend. V and Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 10 by forcing him to choose between invoking the privilege against self-incrimination and contesting a forfeiture. The court declined, however, to strike down the statute as unconstitutional on its face. The court concluded that, by invoking his right against self-incrimination, the claimant lost the ability to intervene in the proceeding, virtually assuring a forfeiture. This effectively constituted an "automatic" economic sanction forbidden by the Fifth Amendment, as it impermissibly forced the claimant to choose between surrendering his constitutional privilege and forfeiting the property. As the claimant had a significant property interest, due process required a meaningful hearing. Acknowledging the state's need to protect against fraudulent claims, the court noted that the property at issue was taken directly from the claimant, thereby meeting the substantive concerns of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4311. Finding that the claimant's dismissal was unduly harsh, the court granted him standing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of forfeiture, vacated the trial court's order, and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

Roosevelt Elementary Sch. Dist. No. 66 v. Bishop


Supreme Court No. CV-93-0168-T/AP


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


179 Ariz. 233; 877 P.2d 806; 1994 Ariz. LEXIS 79; 172 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



July 21, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected September 26, 1994.
Related proceeding at Hull v. Albrecht, 190 Ariz. 520, 950 P.2d 1141, 1997 Ariz. LEXIS 135 (Ariz., 1997)
Related proceeding at Hull v. Albrecht, 192 Ariz. 34, 960 P.2d 634, 1998 Ariz. LEXIS 59 (Ariz., 1998)
Related proceeding at Roosevelt Elem. Sch. Dist. No. 66 v. State, 2003 Ariz. App. LEXIS 126 (Ariz. Ct. App., Aug. 14, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals No. 1 CA-CV 92-504. Maricopa County Superior Court No. CV 91-10387. Appeal from the Superior Court of Arizona in Maricopa County. The Honorable Stanley Z. Goodfarb, Judge



DISPOSITION: REVERSED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, school districts and parents (collectively, districts), brought an action in the Superior Court of Arizona in Maricopa County against defendants, superintendent and State Board of Education (collectively, state), seeking a declaration that the State of Arizona's statutory scheme for financing public education violated Ariz. Const. XI, § 1. The trial court granted summary judgment for the state. The districts appealed.


OVERVIEW: Under the state's scheme for financing public education, Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 15-101 to -1901, the quality of a district's capital was related to the local property tax base. The parties did not dispute that there were enormous disparities in the districts as to the condition of buildings and classroom equipment. The court relied not on the state constitution's equal protection (or privileges and immunities) clause, Ariz. Const. art. II, § 13, but the more specific state constitutional provisions regarding education, Ariz. Const. art. XI, § 1, requiring the state to establish "a general and uniform public school system." The court held that under Ariz. Const. art. XI the state, not the school districts, was obligated to maintain a school system. The requirement of "general and uniform" did not mandate equal funding but only sufficient funds to educate children on substantially equal terms. Disparities among districts caused by local control did not violate art. XI. Because the state's statutory system violated Ariz. Const. art. XI, § 1, as the source of substantial disparities in financing education, the districts were entitled to a declaration that the scheme was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court and remanded for entry of judgment for the districts, declaring that the state's system for financing public education violated the state constitution.

Thurston v. Judges' Retirement Plan


Supreme Court No. CV-93-0451-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


179 Ariz. 49; 876 P.2d 545; 1994 Ariz. LEXIS 62; 167 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 13



June 16, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals No. 1 CA-CV 92-0449. Maricopa County No. CV 91-06288. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable Michael A. Yarnell, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One,    Ariz.   , 868 P.2d 1011 (App. 1993). VACATED



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner widow appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Arizona), which reversed the superior court's award of summary judgment in her claim for surviving spouse benefits under decedent's retirement policy and Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 38-801(C)(amended 1981). Respondent retirement plan argued that the widow was not entitled to the increased benefit enacted by statute after her husband's retirement and before his death.


OVERVIEW: After a state employee retired, the legislature amended the surviving spouse benefit under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 38-801(C). The original scheme gave surviving spouses one-third of the decedent's monthly benefits; the amended scheme gave them a two-thirds share. When the employee's widow applied for the two-thirds benefit, the retirement plan denied the claim and awarded only the benefit in effect before decedent's retirement. The superior court granted the widow's motion for summary judgment, but the court of appeals reversed. The court explained that the widow's benefits had vested at decedent's death and that decedent's benefits had vested when his employment began. Either way, the amended statute applied to increase the widow's benefit. The widow's contingent benefit had not vested at the time of the amendment, which altered a substantive right. Substantive rights could be altered before they vested. Alternately, decedent's benefits were altered after they had vested; however, when the change benefited both decedent and his widow, the change was presumed to be accepted by the beneficiaries. The court vacated the appellate decision and reinstated summary judgment for the widow.


OUTCOME: The court held that the widow was entitled to the increased benefit created by statutory amendment after her husband's retirement and before his death. The court vacated the appellate court's decision and reinstated the trial court's award of summary judgment to the widow.

Ridenour v. Schwartz


Supreme Court No. CV-94-0136-SA


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


179 Ariz. 1; 875 P.2d 1306; 1994 Ariz. LEXIS 60; 166 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 20; 22 Media L. Rep. 1959



June 7, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected June 7, 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Maricopa County. No. CR 93-00399



DISPOSITION: JURISDICTION ACCEPTED. RELIEF GRANTED IN PART, DENIED IN PART


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner criminal accused brought a special action against respondent judge (trial judge) of the Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona), which challenged an administrative order that limited public access to the courthouse to those persons already in the building by three o'clock. The accused sought a stay of his trial or a mistrial.


OVERVIEW: In response to the county board's request to reduce costs, the presiding judge of the county superior court issued the order that restricted public access to the courthouse for the remainder of the fiscal year. When the order took effect, the accused's trial was in its seventh week with proceedings conducted in the afternoon. The trial judge denied the accused's motions for a public trial or, in the alternative, to dismiss and for a stay of the proceedings until the court resolved the matter. In the special action, the court granted a local newspaper's request to appear as amicus curiae on its argument that the order unconstitutionally denied the press and the public access to observe trials. The court recognized the accused's right to a public trial and the public's right to observe court proceedings as guaranteed by the federal and state constitutions. Noting the order's limited effect, the court held that the accused was not denied his right to a public trial and denied his request for a mistrial. However, vacating the order, the court held that it was an unconstitutional attempt to partially close the court, which was not case specific and violated the separation of powers.


OUTCOME: The court denied the accused's request for a mistrial but held that the administrative order restricting public access to the courthouse was unconstitutional and vacated it.

 

72 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Hayes v. Continental Ins. Co.


Supreme Court No. CV-92-0199-PR


SUPREME COURT OF ARIZONA


178 Ariz. 264; 872 P.2d 668; 1994 Ariz. LEXIS 44; 163 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



April 21, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals No. 1 CA-CV 90-600. Maricopa County No. CV 90-90510. Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable David L. Grounds, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 172 Ariz. 573, 838 P.2d 1334 (Ct. App. 1992). VACATED



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant workers' compensation claimant sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals of Maricopa County (Arizona), which affirmed the trial court's judgment dismissing the bad faith action filed by appellant against appellee workers' compensation insurance carrier on the grounds that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 29-30 divested the state court of jurisdiction over such action.


OVERVIEW: Appellant workers' compensation claimant sought compensation benefits, but appellee workers' compensation insurance carrier denied her claim without explanation. Appellant filed an action against appellee, alleging bad faith. The trial court held, and the appellate court affirmed, that the state courts had no jurisdiction to hear appellant's claim and that her exclusive remedy was within the administrative framework of the workers' compensation commission. Appellant sought review. The court reversed and remanded, holding that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 29-30, on which appellee relied, addressed bad faith claims, but the law was ambiguous and required judicial interpretation. The court reviewed the statute's language, history, subject matter, legislative intent, its purpose and spirit and found nothing to indicate that the legislature had intended to preempt judicial jurisdiction over common law causes of action in the workers' compensation arena. The court held that the statute's reference to bad faith claims addressed only administrative complaints made pursuant to its provisions and did not abrogate the common law cause of action for bad faith denial of workers' compensation benefits.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment of dismissal and vacated the appellate court's affirmance, and remanded to the trial court for proceedings in accordance with the court's holding that the legislature had not abrogated common law causes of action for bad faith denial of workers' compensation benefits and that state courts continued to have jurisdiction to hear such actions.

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County. The Honorable Jeffrey A. Hotham, Judge. Maricopa County Superior Court CR-90-03339



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions and sentences for first degree murder stemming from sexual conduct with a minor under age 15, in the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona).


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the evidence was insufficient that the victim was dead during the assault, the instructions were errorneous, especially the aggravating and mitigating factors instruction, evidence was excluded, and DNA evidence was unconstitutional. The court disagreed. It said the uncontradicted expert testimony defeated the argument that the victim was not a person at the time of the sexual penetration; she was alive during penetration. Likewise the court rejected defendant's contention that he had not possessed the mental state of "knowingly" because he believed that the victim was dead at the time of the sexual penetration. The court found sufficient evidence that defendant formed the intent to sexually assault the victim before her death, and overwhelming evidence supported the jury's verdict. The court said the DNA evidence was properly admitted as identity was not at issue, and the parties stipulated to its admission at trial. In addition, although defendant's impairment during the murder failed to rise to the level of a statutorily mitigating circumstance, the trial judge should have considered whether it constituted a non-statutory mitigating circumstance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions but remanded the case for resentencing on the first degree murder conviction.
