Fain Land & Cattle Co. v. Hassell


No. CV-89-0186-SA


Supreme Court of Arizona


163 Ariz. 587; 790 P.2d 242; 1990 Ariz. LEXIS 60; 57 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 19



March 30, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied May 8, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: SPECIAL ACTION.



DISPOSITION: RELIEF DENIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner cattle corporation commenced an action against respondent state land commissioner and state land department seeking to require respondents to complete an exchange of school trust lands for privately held lands pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 37-604 et seq. Respondents claimed that the transaction was not permitted under Ariz. Const. art. 10.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner cattle company and non-party school district sought to exchange school trust lands for privately held lands pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 37-604 et seq. Respondents, state land commissioner and state land department, refused to allow the transfer and claimed that Ariz. Const. art. 10 precluded the transaction. Petitioner commenced the action. The court denied the petition. The court held that the Arizona Constitution limited the disposal of state trust lands to the methods provided by the Enabling Act, Act of June 20, 1910, Pub.L. No. 219 (ch. 310), 36 Stat. 557, and the Arizona Constitution. The court reasoned that under both the Enabling Act and the Arizona Constitution, the state court could not enter into an exchange without complying with the public auction requirement of the Arizona Constitution. The court reasoned that Ariz. Rev. Stat. §37-604 et seq. was unconstitutional as applied to school trust land because an exchange made for a preset appraised monetary value was a form of sale, and no authorization for the exchange was permitted under the Arizona Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner cattle corporation's petition seeking to require respondents, state land commissioner and state land department, to complete an exchange for school trust lands for privately held lands because the statute that permitted the exchange was unconstitutional. The exchange made for a preset appraised monetary value was a form of sale, which was not permitted under the Arizona Constitution.

Big D Constr. Corp. v. Court of Appeals, Div. One


Nos. CV-89-0304-SA, CV-89-0362-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


163 Ariz. 560; 789 P.2d 1061; 1990 Ariz. LEXIS 55; 89 A.L.R.4th 567; 57 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 8



March 28, 1990 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Court of Appeals, No. 1 CV-SA 89-173, Maricopa Court, No. CV 89-21284, SPECIAL ACTION, RELIEF GRANTED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner construction company sought review of respondent lower court's judgment upholding the constitutionality of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 34-241.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner construction company submitted the lowest bid for a city project, but a competing contractor was awarded the bid after it was determined that under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 34-241, Arizona's bid preference statute, the competing contractor was the better bidder. Respondent court denied petitioner's requested relief. Petitioner then filed for review of the lower court's decision and sought a determination of § 34-241 as unconstitutional. The court noted that because petitioner and the competing contractor had settled, the controversy between them was moot, but because the issue was one of public importance that was likely to recur, it considered the petition. After applying the rational basis test, the court concluded that § 34-241 had been so altered by amendment that the financial burden it imposed, the discrimination it effected, and the privileges it conferred were no longer rationally related to any legitimate state purpose. The statute violated the equal protection guarantee of Ariz. Const. art. II, § 13. The judgment of the lower court was vacated.


OUTCOME: The court vacated respondent's lower court's judgment upholding the constitutionality of Arizona's bid preference statute because it failed to further any of the legitimate state interests, was arbitrary, was expensive to the public entities that had to comply with it, and simply wasted taxpayers' money.

 

130 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Dunn v. Carruth


Nos. CV-89-0007-SA, CV-89-0073-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


162 Ariz. 478; 784 P.2d 684; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 228; 49 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 15



December 14, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Special Action from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Cause CV 88-31212, Formerly Pima County No. 248197, The Honorable Marilyn A. Riddel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JURISDICTION ACCEPTED; REMANDED.

Special Action from the superior Court of Maricopa County Cause CV 88-18129, The Honorable Edward C. Rapp, Judge, REVIEW ACCEPTED; REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner injured parties presented special action petitions that challenged the venue rulings of respondent, the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), in the injured parties' actions against real party in interest, the State of Arizona (state). The superior court allowed the state to transfer venue of the injured parties' actions to Maricopa County pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-822(B).


OVERVIEW: Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-822(B) granted the state attorney general discretion, once an action against the state was filed, to change the venue to Maricopa County. The state contended that a transfer to Maricopa County pursuant to § 12-822(B) preempted a later transfer for cause under § 12-406, which allowed for changes of venue if the convenience of witnesses and the ends of justice would have been promoted by the change, or for other good and sufficient cause. The court disagreed and held that the injured parties were not precluded from seeking a venue change for cause under § 12-406 after the state obtained transfer to Maricopa County. The court reasoned that there was no legislative intent in § 12-822(B) to abrogate the general venue statutes in actions against the state and noted that venue was proper in the counties where the injured parties filed their actions. Although § 12-411(A) prohibited more than one change of venue per action, the change of venue at the instance of the state under § 12-822(B) was not encompassed in § 12-411(A)'s restriction. In so ruling, the court did not reach the question of the constitutionality of § 12-822(B). Thus, the court remanded.


OUTCOME: The court remanded both injured parties' actions to the superior court for proceedings not inconsistent with the opinion.

 

131 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Schad


No. 4876-3


Supreme Court of Arizona


163 Ariz. 411; 788 P.2d 1162; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 229; 49 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 23



December 14, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Yavapai, County, Yavapai County Superior Court No. 8752, The Honorable Richard Anderson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and sentenced to death by the Yavapai County Superior Court (Arizona) on remand.


OVERVIEW: The defendant appealed contending that he was denied a fair trial when the state failed to preserve the victim's clothing and fingerprint impressions on items found with the body and that it was improper to use his prior murder conviction as an aggravating factor. The court held that the duty of the State to preserve evidence was limited to evidence that might be expected to play a significant role in the suspect's defense. The court concluded that the chances were extremely low that any of the evidence which was not preserved would have been exculpatory for the defendant. As a result, the court found that he was not so seriously prejudiced by the loss of the evidence that he did not receive a fair trial. The defendant also argued that his previous conviction for second degree murder did not involve violence and that it was an accident involving consenting homosexual partners. The court accepted the trial court's finding that the total mitigation present was not sufficient to overcome any one of the aggravating circumstances, thus, no error occurred.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction and sentence was affirmed.

 

132 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Prentiss


No. CR-88-0148-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


163 Ariz. 81; 786 P.2d 932; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 190; 48 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 17



November 9, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Granted in Part and Denied in Part and Opinion Modified February 15, 1990. *


*  Chief Justice Gordon and Justice Moeller did not participate in the determination of the motion for reconsideration.


PRIOR HISTORY: Petition for Review from the Superior Court of Pima County, Pima County No. CR-20262, The Honorable Philip Fahringer, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, Court of Appeals No. 2 CA-CR 87-0419, Filed February 4, 1988, VACATED.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty of operating a motor vehicle while there was 0.10 percent or more of alcohol concentration in her blood or breath in violation of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 28-692(B). Defendant appealed from a decision of the Court of Appeals (Arizona) which affirmed the conviction and affirmed the sentence, which did not take mitigating circumstances pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 28-692.01(D) into account.


OVERVIEW: The prosecution did not allege the "alternative sentencing provisions" at the time of sentencing pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 28-692.01(C) because defendant rejected the state's plea and elected to exercise her right to a jury trial. Defendant had been driving with less than .20 percent alcohol weight in her blood and had caused no personal injury. The municipal judge sentenced defendant pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 28-692.01(D). The superior court remanded the case on appeal for resentencing. The lower court affirmed the judgment of conviction and a sentence that did not take the mitigating circumstances into account. The court vacated the judgment and reinstate the sentence imposed by the municipal judge. Section 28-692.01(C) required a prosecutorial allegation in order for a judge to apply § 28-692.01(D). The legislature overreached its power given to it in Ariz. Const. art. III. This restricted the power of the judiciary to perform its constitutional mandate. To that extent, § 28-692.01(C) was unconstitutional. The court held that the phrase requiring a prosecutorial allegation be severed therefrom and the balance of the statute remain in full force and effect.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment of the lower court and reinstated the sentence of the municipal judge, which took mitigating circumstances into account on defendant's conviction for driving while operating a motor vehicle while intoxicated.

 

133 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Marlow


No. CR-87-0046-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


163 Ariz. 65; 786 P.2d 395; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 192; 47 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 37



November 9, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Mohave County, Mohave County No. CR-8044, The Honorable Steven F. Conn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTIONS AFFIRMED, SENTENCE MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from his convictions and sentences by the Superior Court of Mohave County (Arizona), which found him guilty of murder in the first degree and armed robbery. Defendant was sentenced to death for the murder and sentenced to a consecutive, aggravated term of 21 years imprisonment for the armed robbery.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the trial court erroneously relied on a prior conviction for assault with intent to commit violence as an aggravating factor, because a probation officer's testimony identifying defendant as the person convicted fell short of proof beyond a reasonable doubt as required by Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-703(C). The court found that the evidence that the killing was committed to avoid detection supported the finding of the aggravating factor of commission of murder for pecuniary gain and the finding of the aggravating factor that the killing was committed in a heinous or depraved manner. However, the court found that that evidence could only be weighed once in evaluating whether the death penalty should be imposed. Therefore, the court found that there was only one substantial aggravating factor to be weighed against a substantial mitigating factor, the dramatic disparity in sentence between defendant's death sentence and co-defendant's four-year prison term, which the trial court failed to weigh. The court concluded that the sentence of death on the murder count should be reduced to life imprisonment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder and armed robbery and his sentence for robbery. The court reduced the death sentence to life imprisonment without possibility of parole for 25 years.

 

134 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Dumaine


No. CR-84-0344-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


162 Ariz. 392; 783 P.2d 1184; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 191; 47 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 24



November 7, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County, Pima County No. A-35088, The Honorable Michael J. Brown.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Superior Court of Pima County, Pima County (Arizona) of first degree murder and armed robbery. Defendant appealed from both convictions and sought review of the trial court's denial of post-conviction relief.


OVERVIEW: The court held, inter alia, that (1) defendant was not deprived of right to present witnesses because there was no per se violation for the trial court judge or the prosecuting attorney to have advised the witness of the penalties for testifying falsely and the witness was available for cross-examination; (2) no prosecutorial misconduct occurred for arranging a favorable plea agreement with one of several witnesses testifying against defendant, and defendant was not denied a fair trial where the jury was able to assess the credibility of that testimony; (3) the prosecutor's comments did not deny defendant a fair trial because the prosecutor did not vouch for the credibility or the truthfulness of the witnesses or imply that the witnesses were credible just because the state had called them; (4) no evidence supported a manslaughter instruction and a lesser included offense instruction should not have been given when the evidence was such that defendant was guilty only of the crime charged or not guilty at all; and (5) a self-defense instruction would have been given only if defendant could have demonstrated the elements of self-defense.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions.

 

135 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Greene


No. CR-88-0208-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


162 Ariz. 431; 784 P.2d 257; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 177; 45 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 8



October 10, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Court of Appeals No. 1 CA-CR 10784, Maricopa County No. CR-154460, The Honorable Bernard J. Dougherty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: SUPPRESSION ORDER VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed a decision of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which granted defendant's suppression order, affirmed by an appellate court that remanded for consideration of whether a police officer acted in good faith. Defendant petitioned the court for review of the good faith issue. The state cross-petitioned, challenging the suppression order.


OVERVIEW: A police officer responding to a domestic violence call was shown to a bathroom where defendant had allegedly done damage during a family fight. Defendant's wife spoke to the officer while he took photographs. When he could no longer hear her, he followed her into the bedroom, where he noticed Hawaiian leis. Recalling a recent sexual assault case in which the assailant fit defendant's description and wore a lei, the officer later relayed the information to his supervisor. Defendant was later arrested on a probation violation and ended up confessing to the sexual assault. A search warrant was issued, and the leis were seized. The trial court suppressed the leis and confession, and an appellate court affirmed, but remanded for consideration of the officer's "good faith." On the parties' petition and cross-petition for review, the court vacated the court of appeals' memorandum, set aside the trial court's suppression order, and remanded. The court found that the suppression was an abuse of the trial court's discretion because the officer had entered the home pursuant to the exigency of the call, and then observed the leis during his routine, and proper, investigation.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the appellate court's memorandum decision and set aside the trial court's suppression order, then remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

136 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Mecham v. Ariz. House of Representatives


Nos. CV-89-0245-SA, CV-89-0246-SA


Supreme Court of Arizona


162 Ariz. 267; 782 P.2d 1160; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 169; 47 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 7



September 29, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] SPECIAL ACTION, JURISDICTION DECLINED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a former governor and others, requested the court to review by special action the impeachment proceedings in the Arizona legislature which led to the removal of the governor from office. Petitioners also sought orders directing the payment of the governor's attorney fees and his recall expenses, and they further sought injunctive and other relief relative to the protocol fund.


OVERVIEW: The legislature impeached the governor and caused him to be removed from office. Petitioners sought review of the impeachment proceedings by special action. They also sought compensation for the governor's attorney fees and recall expenses and injunctive and other relief concerning the protocol fund. The court declined to accept jurisdiction over any of the issues brought before it by petitioners. The impeachment proceedings met all of the requirements contained in Ariz. Const. art. 8, pt. 2, §§ 1, 2. The court found that it lacked authority under the state Constitution to dictate to the state senate how it should carry out its impeachment trial or whether the governor's acts were impeachable. The court noted, however, that the charges preferred against the governor involved a serious abuse of official power and that such charges were generally considered to be impeachable. The court declined to accept jurisdiction on the questions of attorney fees and recall expenses because no statute directed it to authorize payments. Litigation was pending elsewhere regarding the protocol fund, and the court found no justification for intervention.


OUTCOME: The court declined to accept jurisdiction of petitioners' special action that sought review of the governor's impeachment, sought payment of his attorney fees and recall expenses, and sought injunctive and other relief with respect to the protocol fund.

State v. Richey


No. CV-88-0293-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


160 Ariz. 564; 774 P.2d 1354; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 109; 36 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 6



May 25, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Apache County, Court of Appeals 1 CA-CIV 9400, Apache County C-8036, The Honorable John L. Claborne, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Apache County, Court of Appeals (Arizona) that affirmed his conviction for driving without an Arizona driver's license and failure to register a vehicle in Arizona and the grant of attorneys fees under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-341.01(C).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of driving without an Arizona driver's license and failure to register a vehicle in Arizona, in violation of Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 28-302(A), 28-411. He was fined and assessed attorneys' fees. The court of appeals affirmed the decision and awarded the state additional attorneys' fees incurred on appeal. The court granted review to consider whether the trial court erred in awarding attorneys' fees against defendant. The basis for the state's motion for attorneys' fees was Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-341.01(C), which mandated a fee award only upon clear and convincing evidence that the claim or defense constituted harassment, was groundless and not made in good faith. The trial court apparently relied on this subsection in granting the motion, finding that the allegations of the unconstitutionality of Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 28-302(A), 28-411 were frivolous. The trial court neither set forth the evidence it considered nor found that it was clear and convincing. Because the trial court failed to make specific findings as required by Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-341.01(C), the court vacated the trial court's award of attorneys' fees to the state.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the attorneys' fee award to the state, and affirmed the superior court's imposition of fines against defendant. In all other respects, the court affirmed the court of appeals' opinion and remanded the cause to the superior court for enforcement of the judgment.

 

138 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


In re Bowen


No. SB-88-0005-D


Supreme Court of Arizona


160 Ariz. 558; 774 P.2d 1348; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 108; 36 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



May 23, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] ORIGINAL PROCEEDING FOR DISCIPLINARY ACTION, Disciplinary Commission No. 5-1102 & 5-0396, Respondent Publicly Censured.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State Bar of Arizona alleged that respondent attorney had violated the Code of Prof'l Responsibility DR 7-102(A)(1), (2), (8), 1-102(A)(4) by filing a lis pendens in an attempt to collect on an unsecured debt.


OVERVIEW: The attorney, who practiced in the area of collections, was hired to collect an unsecured debt. He filed a civil action against the debtor and recorded a lis pendens on any real property that the debtor might have in the county. The civil action did not affect the debtor's title to any real property. The court publicly censured the attorney for his acts. The attorney had not violated DR 7-102(A)(1) because the attorney had not been motivated merely by a desire to harass or injure. The attorney also had not violated DR 7-102(A)(8). The attorney had, however, violated DR 7-102(A)(2) because he had knowingly advanced a claim that was unwarranted under the law. By recording a lis pendens, the attorney had tried to circumvent the requirements of the Arizona Provisional Remedies Act, Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 12-2401 through 12-2412. The attorney admitted he was aware that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-1191 permitted the recording of a lis pendens only when the action affected the title to real property. The attorney's conduct was not constitutionally protected speech. Public censure was the appropriate sanction because the attorney admitted that his conduct had not been an isolated instance.


OUTCOME: The court publicly censured the attorney and assessed costs against him.

State v. Taylor


No. CR-87-0306-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


160 Ariz. 415; 773 P.2d 974; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 55; 32 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 32



April 11, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended on Grant of Reconsideration June 13, 1989.
Post-conviction relief denied at, Petition granted by State v. Pierce, 2007 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1459 (2007)
Post-conviction relief denied at, Petition granted by State v. Pierce, 2010 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1050 (2010)
Post-conviction relief denied at, Petition granted by State v. Pierce, 2015 Ariz. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1591 (2015)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County, Pima County Nos. CR-20582 and CR-20748, The Honorable Thomas Meehan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED IN PART; REVERSED IN PART; REMANDED IN PART FOR RESENTENCING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Pima County (Arizona), which upheld a jury verdict that found defendant guilty of multiple counts of crimes against children resulting in an aggregate prison term of 2,975 years.


OVERVIEW: Defendant repeatedly engaged in the sexual molestation of children during his lifetime. After defendant and his wife vacated a trailer in which they lived, the landlord and others found numerous sexually explicit photos of children. Some of the children were identified, while others were not. Defendant was indicted on 87 counts of sexual exploitation of a minor, sexual conduct with a minor under 15, and attempted sexual conduct with a minor. Defendant was convicted by a jury and sentenced to an aggregate prison term of 2,975 years. Defendant sought review based on a cruel and unusual punishment argument. The court partly reversed by vacating a count that was not presented to the grand jury, otherwise the convictions were affirmed. The court held that the incarceration of defendant for the rest of his life, was not cruel and unusual in light of the serious gravity of the offenses.


OUTCOME: The court partly reversed by vacating a count that was not presented to the grand jury, otherwise defendant's convictions and sentences for sex crimes against children were affirmed.

State v. McCall


No. CR-86-0038-AP/PC


Supreme Court of Arizona


160 Ariz. 119; 770 P.2d 1165; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 27; 29 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 4



March 7, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Nos. CR-116940, CR-120721 (Consolidated), The Honorable Paul W. LaPrade, Judge (Deceased) and The Honorable Edmund G. Noyes, Jr.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which denied him post-conviction relief on his equal protection claim, sentenced him to death, and denied his third petition for post-conviction relief.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first-degree murder and other felonies for participating in a murder for hire with a co-defendant. The trial court denied defendant post-conviction relief and sentenced him to death. On appeal, the court affirmed. Defendant was not denied equal protection due to the fact that his co-defendant received a separate trial after the original trial and defendant did not. Their defenses were not mutually exclusive and the co-defendant was granted a severance because evidence properly elicited by defendant's attorney about his links to organized crime and other "hits" was so prejudicial that the co-defendant was entitled to a separate trial. The trial court properly applied the crime committed for pecuniary gain and especially heinous, cruel, or depraved aggravated circumstances during sentencing. The court refused to allow defendant to conduct an evidentiary hearing or deposition to inquire into the competency of the trial court judge, who underwent brain surgery two days after the trial and subsequently died, as defendant failed to present evidence showing he was incompetent.


OUTCOME: The judgment denying defendant post-conviction relief and sentencing him to death was affirmed.

 

141 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Mountain States Tel. & Tel. Co. v. Arizona Corp. Comm'n


No. CV-88-0330-SA


Supreme Court of Arizona


160 Ariz. 350; 773 P.2d 455; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 24; 28 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



February 14, 1989 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Supplemental Opinion June 1, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: SPECIAL ACTION RELIEF GRANTED


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner telephone company brought an original special action that challenged the constitutionality of an order of respondent, the Arizona Corporation Commission, which required universal blocking of a telephone information service and the submission of a presubscription plan for approval. The providers of the telephone information service also sought to intervene.


OVERVIEW: After the legislature passed Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-2920, the Commission ordered the telephone company to block the telephone information service, which provided customers with access to a message network, and to propose a presubscription plan. The request for special action was granted because the dispute involved fundamental rights under Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 6, and the First Amendment. The providers' motion to intervene was granted because the Commission's presubscription requirement affected their economic interests and fundamental rights. Employing judicial restraint, the court focused on the state constitutional free speech issue. It held that the telephone company had third party standing to assert the providers' rights where it challenged an order as overbroad. The presubscription requirement violated the right to freely speak as guaranteed by Ariz. Const. art. 2, § 6, which was a right not subject to being balanced against the government's interests. Nor was the order a valid time, place, and manner restriction because it was not drawn with narrow specificity. Based on the foregoing, the court declared the order void.


OUTCOME: The court granted the request of the telephone company and the providers for special relief from the Commission's order.

State v. White


No. CR-86-0389-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


160 Ariz. 24; 770 P.2d 328; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 22; 27 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 37



February 9, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Maricopa County Superior Court No. CR-156030, The Honorable Stephen J. Ventre, Judge Pro Tempore.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his judgments of conviction and sentences imposed by the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) for the offenses of second degree burglary and sexual assault.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted, and evidence of two prior convictions was submitted for purposes of sentencing. He was sentenced to 20 years of imprisonment on the burglary conviction to run consecutively to a mandatory life sentence on the sexual assault conviction. He appealed the convictions and sentences. The court affirmed both. There was sufficient evidence to support the finding that he had two prior felony convictions. There was no error in the denial of defendant's request for a physical lineup especially in light of the fact that the assailant's identity was never really in issue. Defendant's decision not to testify waived any error with regard to the trial court's ruling to allow his prior convictions to be used for impeachment purposes. The omission of an instruction on the presumption of innocence did not constitute fundamental error. Because defendant attempted to flee before the officers addressed him, the warrantless entry into his home was justified as an exigent circumstance. The consecutive sentences did not constitute double punishment. Defendant was not denied equal protection of the law due to the enhanced sentence he received based on his probationary status.


OUTCOME: The judgments of conviction and the sentences were affirmed.

State v. Walton


No. CR-87-0022-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


159 Ariz. 571; 769 P.2d 1017; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 20; 27 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 21



February 2, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County, Pima County Superior Court No. CR-17537, The Honorable Thomas Meehan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from a judgment of the Superior Court of Pima County (Arizona), which convicted him of first degree murder, armed robbery, kidnapping, and theft by control, and which imposed a death sentence as well as prison terms. Defendant raised many issues on appeal, including his lack of competency evaluation, erroneous voir dire and jury instructions, improper imposition of prison terms, and erroneous evidentiary rulings.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder, armed robbery, kidnapping, and theft by control, and was sentenced to prison terms and to death. He sought review, and the court affirmed. The court held that a preliminary report by a psychologist properly established that defendant was competent to stand trial under Ariz. R. Crim. P. 11 and that there was no abuse of discretion in denying continuances. The court further held that the state was not obligated to elect between premeditated and felony murder, that defendant's statements made during police interrogation were voluntary in that the police statements were not promissory in nature, and did not induce defendant to make a statement. The court also held that there were no errors in voir dire that would have required a mistrial, and that evidentiary rulings were properly made. The court held that jury instructions were proper, that the death penalty procedure was properly followed, and that there were sufficient aggravating factors and insufficient mitigating factors to uphold the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder, armed robbery, kidnapping, and theft by control, and sentences of incarceration and of death. The court reviewed numerous allegations of error and found all lacked merit. The court held that jury instructions, evidentiary rulings, challenges to voir dire, evaluation of death penalty, evidence of competency, and imposition of prison terms were all properly done.

State v. Vickers


No. CR-86-0147-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


159 Ariz. 532; 768 P.2d 1177; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 17; 27 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



January 31, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Pinal County, Cause No. CR-9934, The Honorable James E. Don.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Superior Court of Pinal County (Arizona), which convicted him of first degree murder, in violation of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1105(A)(1), and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a prisoner on death row, set fire to a fellow inmate and killed him. He was convicted of murder and sentenced to death. On appeal, the court affirmed and held, inter alia, that: (1) the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for further diagnostic testing; (2) the trial court did not err in ruling that the prosecutor could have impeached defense witnesses with evidence of their membership in the Aryan Brotherhood, an organization that allegedly required its members to commit perjury, if necessary, to protect another member of the organization; (3) statements made by defendant immediately after the fire fell under the public safety exception to Miranda; (4) defendant's bad acts were relevant because they tended to show that any violence committed by him was not caused by epilepsy; (5) the evidence did not support a manslaughter instruction, and even if the trial court erred by failing to give a manslaughter instruction, the error was harmless, because the jury, though instructed on the lesser-included offense of second degree murder, still convicted defendant of first degree murder; and (6) the trial court did not err in sentencing defendant to death.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's first degree murder conviction and death sentence.

 

145 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Vincent


No. CR-87-0001-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


159 Ariz. 418; 768 P.2d 150; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 13; 26 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 33



January 19, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County, No. CR-17077, The Honorable Jack T. Arnold, The Honorable Raner C. Collins.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence for murder in the first degree by the Superior Court of Pima County Arizona. That court allowed child witnesses to testify via videotape without a showing of particularized need as required under Az. Rev. Stat. § 13-4251 et seq., and overruled defendant's objections regarding prosecutor's improper vouching for the honesty of its witnesses.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the prosecutor improperly vouched for his witnesses' credibility, and defendant's federal and state constitutional right to confront adverse witnesses was violated by the state's videotaped presentation of the testimony of his minor children. The court affirmed. The court held that because the prosecutor's arguments vouched for the honesty of its witness, the argument was absolutely improper. The court held that the test required courts to determine the likelihood that a jury was affected by the state's remarks. However, because vouching was neither an invited nor an acceptable response, and when the state resorted to vouching for the credibility of a witness, it seriously jeopardized the validity of the conviction that it sought. In addition, the court said that Az. Rev. Stat. § 13-4251 et seq., was unconstitutionally applied, where the state was permitted to shield the children from face-to-face confrontational testimony without first making an individualized showing that either of them needed such protection, and without such a showing, the error required reversal as defendant's Sixth Amendment right was violated.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was reversed, and the case was remanded for a new trial.

 

146 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


Jung v. Phoenix


No. CV-87-0199-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


160 Ariz. 38; 770 P.2d 342; 1989 Ariz. LEXIS 1; 26 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 11



January 12, 1989 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court of Maricopa County, Court of Appeals No. 1 CA-CIV 8692, Honorable James Moeller, Judge. Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, Maricopa County Superior Court No. C-543809, Filed February 24, 1987.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city and city and water department officials, petitioned for review of an order of the Court of Appeals (Arizona), which reversed the trial court's judgment of dismissal for the city in plaintiff nonresidents' action filed under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The action sought damages and injunctive relief due to alleged discrimination by the city in charging different water rates for customers residing outside its exterior boundaries.


OVERVIEW: The city enacted an ordinance that doubled the water rates for those residing outside the geographical boundaries of the city. The court reversed and remanded the case to the trial court. The court reasoned that the nonresidents did not have a civil rights action against the city. The nonresidents had no constitutional right to receive water at a particular rate and their remedy was under state law. Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 9-516 prohibited a city from discontinuing water services to nonresidents. The court held that the implication of reasonable rates for utility service must have been read into § 9-516 and the city must provide water service at a reasonable rate. As a general rule, a municipally owned waterworks system that supplies water outside its corporate limits may have charged more for that service than it charges the users who resided within the corporate limits. Proof that service of nonresidents involved greater expenses was sufficient to show that a city acted reasonably in charging high rates for nonresidents. The enactment of the new statute, § 9-511(A), only affected the nonresidents in their damage claim for the time after the effective date of the amendment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court of dismissal for the city. The court remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

147 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Stevens


No. CR-86-0325-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


158 Ariz. 595; 764 P.2d 724; 1988 Ariz. LEXIS 180



November 3, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE SUPERIOR COURT OF MARICOPA COUNTY, Maricopa County No. CR-146348, The Honorable Bernard J. Dougherty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: CONVICTIONS AFFIRMED; SENTENCE MODIFIED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which convicted him of first-degree murder, attempted first-degree murder, armed robbery, and possession of dangerous drugs. The trial court sentenced defendant to death for the murder conviction and imposed aggravated consecutive prison terms for the attempted murder and armed robbery convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of several violent felonies. On appeal, defendant claimed that his Fifth and Sixth Amendment rights were violated, the state capital punishment statute was unconstitutional, and that the trial court abused its discretion, committed reversible error, and improperly imposed the death penalty. The court held that (1) defendant's Sixth Amendment rights were not violated because he failed to establish the existence of an agency relationship between the jailhouse informant and the police; (2) the trial court neither abused its discretion, nor committed reversible error; (3) the trial court properly considered the victim impact statements; and (4) the trial court erred when it imposed the death penalty because it incorrectly determined that there was no mitigating circumstance sufficiently substantial to call for leniency. Defendant drank heavily and ingested drugs prior to the shooting. Defendant's impaired condition, the psychological reports, and no prior convictions were sufficiently substantial to outweigh the aggravating factor of pecuniary gain..


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions, but vacated the death sentence.

State v. King


No. CR-86-0063-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


158 Ariz. 419; 763 P.2d 239; 1988 Ariz. LEXIS 151; 17 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 46



September 22, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, No. CR-145870, The Honorable Jeffrey S. Cates, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which convicted defendant of first degree murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged an order which convicted him of first degree murder. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for a new trial, holding that the trial court erred in giving the instructions that it did. The court concluded that an insanity instruction materially misstated defendant's burden on the primary issue. The court held that the instruction went to the very heart and foundation of defendant's case, damaging that case on a matter essential to the defense and casting doubt on the integrity of the verdict. The trial court did not help the fundamental error problem or render it "harmless" by noting for the jury that the clear and convincing standard was a lesser burden of proof than the state's burden of proving all the elements of the crime beyond a reasonable doubt. The correct instruction would have apprised the jury that the clear and convincing standard was an intermediate standard, between proof beyond a reasonable doubt and proof by a preponderance of the evidence, and that clear and convincing evidence was evidence that made the existence of the issue propounded "highly probable."


OUTCOME: The court reversed an order convicting defendant of first degree murder and remanded the cause to the trial court for a new trial.

State v. Brita

158 Ariz. 121; 761 P.2d 1025; 1988 Ariz. LEXIS 140; 16 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 10

 Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One, 154 Ariz. 517, 744 P.2d 429 (App. 1987), Vacated in Part, Approved in Part.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which granted defendant's motion to suppress the results of a blood test.

Defendant told the officer he had been drinking, but the officer did not arrest defendant at the time. 151 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Fulminante


No. CR-86-0053-AP


Supreme Court of Arizona


161 Ariz. 237; 778 P.2d 602; 1988 Ariz. LEXIS 81; 38 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 16



June 16, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Granted April 11, 1989. Supplemental Opinion July 11, 1989. Reconsideration Denied September 19, 1989.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, No. CR-142821, The Honorable Stephen A. Gerst.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was indicted for first degree murder under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-1105(A)(1) and filed a motion to suppress evidence, which was denied by the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona). After a jury trial, defendant was found guilty and sentenced to death under Ariz. Rev. Stat. §13-703. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of strangling then shooting his stepdaughter and possibly sexually assaulting the 11-year-old child first. The court reviewed the record for fundamental error pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4035 and found none. No Miranda warning was required when a paid informant questioned defendant while in prison on other charges because there was no objective indicia of arrest and the murder investigation was not yet focused on defendant. The confession to said informant should have been suppressed as coercive, for offering protection from other inmates, but such was harmless error because the confession to the informant's girlfriend was remote in time from the first and made after defendant's prison release and the girlfriend was not a state agent. The death sentence was properly imposed because the record revealed aggravating factors of cruelty from the form of the murder and depravity and heinousness from the confessed statements about defendant's feelings toward the victim. The terms cruel, heinous, and depraved are not vague rendering the death penalty statute unconstitutional. Defendant did not have a right to a voir dire examination of the trial judge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the conviction and judgment of death.

State v. Allen


No. CR-87-0087-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


157 Ariz. 165; 755 P.2d 1153; 1988 Ariz. LEXIS 78; 9 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 8



June 2, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County, Court of Appeals No. 2 CA-CR 4376 & 2 CA-CR 4695-2PR (Consolidated), Pima County Superior Court No. CR-15802, The Honorable John G. Hawkins, Judge.

Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, Filed February 26, 1987, VACATED.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals, Division Two (Arizona), which affirmed the trial court's decision convicting defendant of one count of child molestation and sentencing him to seven years' imprisonment, on the grounds that the trial court erred in ruling that the child victim was unavailable and that admission of expert testimony attesting to the child's credibility was fundamental error.


OVERVIEW: The child victim was under observation for signs of sexual abuse when she reported the alleged incident, and the record indicated that she had suffered prior sexual abuse at the hands of her father and others. The court found that defendant waived the issue of the child's unavailability to testify by failing to object at trial. However, the court concluded that the admission of the child's hearsay statements was fundamental error because her hearsay statements were inadmissible and there was no evidence without her statements to convict defendant. The court found that the child's statements to her mother, police, and her therapists were not admissible under some recognized or residual exception to the hearsay rule and there was inadequate corroborative evidence to bolster the reliability of her statements to satisfy the residual exception. The court found that the child's advanced knowledge of sexual matters could not be solely attributed to the alleged incident between her and the defendant, which negated the reliability that such knowledge ordinarily provided. Defendant's ambiguous response to the mother's accusation was insufficient to corroborate the child's statements.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the appellate court's decision, reversed defendant's conviction, and remanded the case for a new trial.

State v. Johnson


No. CR-87-0117-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


156 Ariz. 464; 753 P.2d 154; 1988 Ariz. LEXIS 45; 6 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 3



April 19, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Pinal County, Pinal County No. CR-11303, The Honorable James E. Don, Judge.

Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two,     Ariz.    ,     P.2d     (App.    ), Court of Appeals No. 2 CA-CR 4729, VACATED.



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Court of Appeals, Division Two (Arizona), which ordered defendant released unconditionally unless within thirty days the state was successful in placing him with a facility pursuant to the terms of the trial court's conditional release order.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found not guilty by reason of insanity and committed him to the state hospital. The trial court concluded that defendant no longer suffered from a mental disease or defect and ordered him conditionally released pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. §§ 13-3994(C), 36-540.01. The trial court denied defendant's request for reconsideration. The court of appeals ordered defendant released unconditionally unless within thirty days the state was successful in placing him with a facility pursuant to the terms of the trial court's conditional release order. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, vacated the opinion of the appellate court, and remanded. The court held that a person who had a mental disease or defect was still "suffering" from it within the meaning of Ariz. Rev. There was no support in the record for the trial court's finding that defendant no longer suffered from a mental disease or defect. The court held the failure to demonstrate defendant would follow an outpatient program rendered him ineligible for conditional release under Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 36-540.01(A).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and vacated the opinion of the court of appeals. The court remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

Barsema v. Susong


No. CV-87-0031-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


156 Ariz. 309; 751 P.2d 969; 1988 Ariz. LEXIS 43; 3 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 21



March 10, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] APPEAL FROM THE SUPERIOR COURT OF MARICOPA COUNTY, Cause No. C500504, Honorable Paul W. LaPrade, Judge (Deceased).



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff husband and wife appealed from the judgment of the court of appeals (Arizona), which held that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-569 was unconstitutional, but which affirmed the trial court's judgment for plaintiffs in their malpractice action against defendant doctor on the ground that the trial court's error in limiting plaintiffs' cross-examination of an expert witness in accordance with this statute was harmless.


OVERVIEW: One of the doctor's expert witnesses was a vice president of the malpractice insurer that had issued the doctor's malpractice policy. Basing his ruling on Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-569, which prohibited the introduction of evidence of malpractice insurance, the trial judge limited cross-examination of the expert witness to preclude any mention of the insurer. The court held that § 12-569 was unconstitutional, but that the trial judge's error in limiting cross-examination was harmless. The Arizona Supreme Court had adopted the Arizona Rules of Evidence pursuant to its constitutional rulemaking power. These rules set the framework for determining what was relevant evidence. The separation of powers doctrine prohibited the legislature from defining what was relevant evidence. Here, the statute forbade the introduction of the evidence no matter how relevant or how nonprejudicial it might be. The court held that appellants in this case bore the burden of proving that the trial court's decision was prejudicial. Given the number of experts who testified for the defense, they failed to demonstrate that they were prejudiced by the error limiting cross-examination of one expert.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.

Bryant v. Continental Conveyor & Equip. Co.


No. CV-86-0525-CQ


Supreme Court of Arizona


156 Ariz. 193; 751 P.2d 509; 1988 Ariz. LEXIS 39; 2 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 8; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P11,683



February 24, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied April 5, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Questions of Law Certified By The United States District Court for the District of Arizona, The Honorable Richard M. Bilby, United States District Court Judge, QUESTIONS ANSWERED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner injured consumer brought an action against defendant manufacturer and others for negligence, gross negligence, and strict products liability. The United States District Court for the District of Arizona certified a question of law to determine whether the Arizona statute of repose in a strict products liability case, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-551, violated Ariz. Const. art. 18, § 6.


OVERVIEW: The consumer was injured while operating a cotton gin that was distributed for ultimate use or consumption more than 12 years before the action accrued. The manufacturer alleged an affirmative defense that the Arizona statute of repose was applicable to strict products liability cases. The federal court certified the question to determine the constitutionality of the statute. The court held the statute of repose was not an abrogation of a substantive right of action protected by Ariz. Const. art. 18, § 6 because the tort of strict products liability did not exist at the time the constitutional provision was adopted. Although a right of access to the courts was a "fundamental" right that, if infringed, demanded as justification a compelling state interest, the court held that the consumer was not denied access to the courts in the constitutional sense. Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-551 satisfied the rational basis test and was constitutional under the equal protection and due process clauses of the Arizona Constitution. The classification created by the statute was reasonably related to the legislature's purpose in dealing with what was perceived in 1977 to be a products liability crisis.


OUTCOME: The court answered "no" to the certified question and held that the Arizona statute of repose in a strict products liability case was constitutional.

State v. Tocco


No. CR-87-0032-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


156 Ariz. 116; 750 P.2d 874; 1988 Ariz. LEXIS 40; 2 Ariz. Adv. Rep. 5



February 23, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, The Honorable Robert S. Hertzberg, Judge.

Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division One,    Ariz.   ,    P.2d    (1987) Approved as Supplemented



DISPOSITION: REVERSED AND REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Court of Appeals, Division One (Arizona), which reversed the trial court's order of dismissal of the charge against him for leading organized crime based on Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-2308 being unconstitutionally vague. He argued that the pre-amendment § 13-2308(A)(1) contained an insufficient scienter requirement and the definition of criminal syndicate was imprecise.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with 13 counts, including one count of leading organized crime in violation of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-2308. The trial court concluded that prior to its 1985 amendment, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-2308 was unconstitutionally vague and granted his motion to dismiss on that basis. The appellate court reversed. The court affirmed. Nowhere did defendant claim that his potentially innocent conduct was being prosecuted, so he lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute. But the court found that even if he had standing, there was no merit in the substance of his constitutional challenge. Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-2301(C)(2), defining "criminal syndicate," was sufficiently precise as to avoid criminalizing entirely innocent behavior. Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-2308(A)(1), prior to its amendment in 1985, was not unconstitutionally vague for lack of a scienter requirement. The court found that the pre-amendment statute did, in fact, contain an implied scienter requirement as mandated by Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-202(A).


OUTCOME: The appellate decision reversing the trial court's dismissal of one count against the defendant was affirmed as supplemented. Defendant lacked standing to challenge the constitutionality of the statute. Even if he had standing, there was no merit in his constitutional challenge. Neither the pre-amendment statute nor the definition of criminal syndicate was unconstitutionally vague. The matter was remanded to the superior court.

Kadish v. Arizona State Land Dep't


No. CV-86-0238-T


Supreme Court of Arizona


155 Ariz. 484; 747 P.2d 1183; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 237



December 10, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied February 2, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, The Honorable John Sticht, Judge, No. 1 CA-CIV 8616, No. C-433745.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Maricopa County, which granted summary judgment in favor of respondent Arizona State Land Department in petitioners' action seeking a declaration that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 27-234(B) was void and seeking special action relief.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner taxpayers filed an action against respondent Arizona State Land Department seeking a declaration that Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 27-234(B) was void and seeking special action relief. The trial court granted summary judgment to respondent, and the court reversed and remanded the case. The court held that the fixed royalty provisions of § 27-234(B) that permitted respondent to lease minerals on a flat rate royalty violated the appraisal and true value provisions of the Arizona Enabling Act (act) and the comparable provisions of the state constitution. The court held that the amendments to the act did not permit the state to deal with mineral deposits on school trust lands in an unrestricted manner and did not withdraw the stringent dispositional requirements. The court found that the Jones Act was intended to prohibit the sale of mineral deposits, while still allowing their leasing on a royalty basis for terms of five years or less. The court held that the Enabling Act and Ariz. Const. art. 10 forbade the state from making nonhydrocarbon mineral leases without appraisal or for less than their true value and that § 27-234 was unconstitutional and void as to such leases.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's decision to grant summary judgment to respondent Arizona State Land Department. The court granted summary judgment to petitioner taxpayers and held that the fixed royalty statute was unconstitutional and invalid in regard to nonhydrocarbon mineral leases. The court held that the Enabling Act was intended to severely circumscribe the state government's power to deal with the school trust's assets.

State v. Williams


No. 6275


Supreme Court of Arizona


166 Ariz. 132; 800 P.2d 1240; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 204



October 8, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Supplemental Motion for Reconsideration Denied December 4, 1990.
Writ of certiorari denied Williams v. Arizona, 500 U.S. 929, 111 S. Ct. 2043, 114 L. Ed. 2d 128, 1991 U.S. LEXIS 2755, 59 U.S.L.W. 3770 (1991)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Williams v. Stewart, 2006 U.S. App. LEXIS 7550 (9th Cir. Ariz., Mar. 28, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, The Honorable J. D. Howe, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona) entered judgment upon defendant's conviction for first-degree murder and first-degree burglary. Defendant was sentenced to death for the murder conviction and to an aggravated term of 14 years for the burglary conviction. The matter was before the court on automatic appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot an elderly man to death when the man entered his neighbor's house, which defendant was burglarizing. Defendant represented himself at trial and was convicted and sentenced to death. The court affirmed. There was no error in the pre-trial identification of defendant because, although the circumstances surrounding the identification created a substantial likelihood of misidentification, there was no state involvement where defendant insisted on the face-to-face encounter. Defendant was properly denied mental examinations because there was nothing to suggest that he was insane when he killed the victim or that he was incompetent to stand trial. The aggravating favor of murder for pecuniary gain was supported because no other explanation for the killing existed other than the victim's discovery of a burglary in progress. The trial court properly considered defendant's mitigating and aggravating factors, and the capital sentence was not improper. The court had already rejected each of defendant's death penalty constitutional arguments in earlier decisions. Finally, the death penalty was proportional to the crime, given defendant's two prior murder convictions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder and burglary in the first-degree and the sentences of death and 14 years, respectively.

State v. Moorman


No. 6558


Supreme Court of Arizona


154 Ariz. 578; 744 P.2d 679; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 203



October 6, 1987

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Pinal County, The Honorable Richard N. Roylston, Judge, No. CR-11043



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Pinal County, Arizona, convicted defendant of first degree murder. Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that he was arrested without probable cause and that the fruits of his illegal arrest should have been suppressed. The court held that the police had probable cause to arrest defendant at the time he made the statements. The officer did not correctly complete the portion of the search warrant that contained the description of the items to be seized. However, because the affidavit, which properly listed the items to be seized, and warrant were kept together, the defective warrant was saved. The court held that while the search was overbroad and the trial court admitted items not listed in the affidavit, any error was harmless. The court held that the Fourth Amendment did not apply to prison cells. The court held that the Miranda warning given to defendant adequately conveyed the message that defendant had the right to an attorney before he answered any questions. Because defendant refused to stipulate to the victim's identity, the admission of photographs was proper. The court held that the decision not to request instructions on second degree murder was strategic and that the evidence supported aggravating circumstances that supported the death penalty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

154 Ariz. 483; 744 P.2d 10; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 195

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State and defendant filed petitions for review of a decision by the Court of Appeals (Arizona), which vacated a concurrent 21 year sentence imposed by a trial court on defendant for his conviction for attempted second degree murder.


OVERVIEW: The trial court sentenced defendant to aggravated concurrent terms of 21 years for attempted second degree murder and 15 years for aggravated assault. On appeal, he contested neither the assault conviction and sentence nor the attempted second degree murder conviction. Defendant challenged his attempted second degree murder sentence asserting that his term constituted cruel and unusual punishment in violation of the Eighth Amendment. The court of appeals held that any part of defendant's sentence for attempted second degree murder that exceeded the maximum 20 years for second degree murder was unconstitutional, and remanded for resentencing to a term less than 20 years. Stat. § 13-1001(C)(1) carried a heavier penalty than that for attempted second degree murder. Unlike a conviction for second degree murder, defendant had the possibilities of commutation of sentence, pardon, parole, and earned release credits.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the court of appeal's decision and affirmed the sentence of the trial court.

James v. Phoenix Gen. Hosp.


Nos. CV 86-0535-PR, CV 86-0543-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


154 Ariz. 594; 744 P.2d 695; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 196



October 1, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Court of Appeals Nos. 1 CA-CIV 8251, 1 CA-CIV 8840, Maricopa County Nos. C-499758, C-499757, The Honorable Howard V. Peterson, Judge, The Honorable John R. Sticht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated appeal, petitioner survivors sought relief of the decisions of the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Court of Appeals (Arizona), which awarded summary judgment to defendant healthcare providers after ruling that the claims were time barred.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff survivors brought wrongful death actions against defendant healthcare providers alleging medical malpractice. In both cases, the trial court awarded summary judgment and the appellate court affirmed the judgment awards. The court consolidated the appeals and reversed the decisions except for an ancillary complaint against one defendant hospital. The court first held that the malpractice statute, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-564, with its three-year statute of limitations, encompassed death as well as injury, finding no legislative intent for the statute to apply only to injury. The court also found that the generic statute of limitations excepted out § 12-564, so that the generic wrongful death statute, with its two-year statute of limitations did not apply to a medical malpractice suit. Next, the court held that the accrual period began to run at the time of death, citing California and Nevada judicial interpretations of similarly constructed medical malpractice statutes. Thus, the wrongful death actions accrued on the date of the death of their decedents, giving petitioners three years from that date within which to file their actions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments against petitioner survivors because the medical malpractice statute, with its three-year statute of limitations, encompassed death as well as injury, and because the wrongful death actions accrued on the date of the death of petitioners' decedents, giving petitioners three years from that date within which to file their actions.

 

164 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Superior Court


No. CV 87-00062-SA


Supreme Court of Arizona


154 Ariz. 574; 744 P.2d 675; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 198



October 1, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied November 17, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: SPECIAL ACTION, Pima County No. CR-19206, Tucson City Court Nos. 1408997, 1408998 1408999.



DISPOSITION: REMANDED WITH DIRECTIONS


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed a judgment from Superior Court, Pima County (Arizona), which reversed a municipal court's conviction of defendant for driving while under the influence of intoxicating liquor.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, at the time of his arrest for driving while under the influence, was not advised that his refusal to take a chemical breath test could be used as evidence against him in a criminal prosecution. Defendant refused to submit to the chemical breath test, and testimony as to his refusal was admitted into evidence. The court reversed the appellate decision that had found the admission of the evidence of refusal to be erroneous. The court held that Ariz. Const. art. 6, § 5(5) granted to the Arizona Supreme Court the power to make rules relative to all procedural matters in any court. Hence, the statute allowing admission in a criminal trial of evidence regarding an accused's refusal to take a breath test, Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 28-692(K), exceeded the legislature's power. The court found no constitutional error in the trial court's admission of such evidence, however. The court held that refusal to take a chemical breath test was not testimonial evidence but physical evidence only; therefore, it did not violate an accused's self-incrimination rights and was admissible at a criminal trial for driving under the influence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's judgment and remanded with directions to reinstate the judgment of conviction.

 

165 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Hurley


No. 6674


Supreme Court of Arizona


154 Ariz. 124; 741 P.2d 257; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 175



July 2, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, No. CR-146640, The Honorable Cheryl K. Hendrix, Judge.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from the judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which, in a jury trial, convicted defendant of seven counts armed robbery, one count aggravated assault, and sentenced him under the enhancement provisions of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-604.02(A).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted by a jury of seven counts armed robbery and one count aggravated assault. Defendant was sentenced under the enhancement provisions of Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-604.02(A). Defendant sought review, contending that the statute was unconstitutional unless interpreted to require proof beyond a reasonable doubt and trial to a jury, rather than a judge, to determine release status. The court affirmed and found that the trial court determined that defendant was on release from confinement when he committed the offenses. Although the court noted that it had no definite tests to apply in resolving the federal constitutional issue, it was able to conclude from caselaw that there was no requirement to find that release status was a separate element of a separate crime. The court held that release status was a sentence-enhancing factor which may have been found by the judge and need not have been proved beyond a reasonable doubt. The court, having reviewed the record for fundamental error pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 13-4035, found no fundamental error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the application of the sentence enhancement employed by the trial court.

Gosewisch v. American Honda Motor Co.


No. 18249-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


153 Ariz. 400; 737 P.2d 376; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 159; 83 A.L.R.4th 53; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P11,531



April 23, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied June 16, 1987. Supplemental Opinion June 17, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Superior Court of Pima County, No. 199193, The Honorable Richard N. Roylston, Judge.

Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two, No. 2 CA-CIV 5210, Filed April 24, 1985, Vacated in Part.



DISPOSITION: REMANDED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant purchaser sought review of a decision by the Court of Appeals Division Two (Arizona), which affirmed a verdict in favor of appellee manufacturer in the purchaser's products liability action against the manufacturer.


OVERVIEW: The buyer was injured while riding a bike that he purchased from his cousin without the instruction manual. The buyer contended that the lower court erred in not giving the failure to warn jury instruction, and that the jury instruction given pursuant to Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-683(3) was unconstitutional. The court held that: (1) the buyer did not establish a prima facie failure to warn case because evidence that he had not read the manual or previously observed the safety rules precluded a finding that a warning would have changed his behavior; (2) the constitutional guarantee in Ariz. Const. art. XVIII, § 5 is inapplicable to a jury instruction based on Ariz. Rev. Stat. § 12-683(3) because the statutory defenses did not encompass contributory negligence; and (3) the jury instruction predicated on § 12-683(3) was erroneous because it did not convey that use contrary to express instructions must be the "proximate cause" of the purchaser's injuries.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in part and remanded.

Batton v. Tennessee Farmers Mut. Ins. Co.


No. CV-86-0392-PR


Supreme Court of Arizona


153 Ariz. 268; 736 P.2d 2; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 157



April 16, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, No. C-520706, The Honorable Howard V. Peterson, Judge.

DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insured appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which granted defendant insurer's motion to dismiss insured's claim for the reimbursement of medical benefits for lack of personal jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: Insured purchased automobile insurance from insurer while he was a Tennessee resident. Insured was severely injured while riding as a passenger in his brother's car. Insured's Arizona attorney requested medical benefits from insurer. Insurer responded by requesting formal proof of loss and other information. Eventually, insurer cancelled the policy and denied insured's claim for reimbursement. Insured sued insurer in Arizona, alleging breach of contract and bad faith. Insurer had no offices or agents in Arizona, was not licensed to do business in Arizona, and had never investigated, adjusted, settled, or defended a claim in Arizona. The trial court granted insurer's motion to dismiss for lack of personal jurisdiction. The court held that the Due Process Clause barred Arizona state court jurisdiction over insurer because it did not purposefully establish minimum contacts with Arizona.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed, and the decision of the appellate court was approved as modified.

State v. Robinson

Supreme Court of Arizona

Opinion of the Court of Appeals, Division Two,      Ariz.     ,      P.2d      (1986) No. 2 CA-CR 3985, Affirmed as Modified.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Cochise County (Arizona), which convicted him of sexual conduct with a minor and child molestation.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant challenged the introduction of certain out-of-court statements as hearsay. 169 of 1485 DOCUMENTS


State v. Jensen


No. 2908-4


Supreme Court of Arizona


153 Ariz. 171; 735 P.2d 781; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 154



March 27, 1987 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Superior Court of Maricopa County, Cause No. CR 75687, The Honorable Cecil Patterson.



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Maricopa County (Arizona), which convicted him of two counts of first-degree murder and sentenced him to two concurrent life sentences. Defendant was previously convicted but was granted post-conviction relief on the basis that Post Traumatic Stress Disorder (PTSD) was classified as a diagnostic mental disorder subsequent to the first trial. Defendant was a Vietnam War veteran.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the introduction of a witness's testimony from the first trial did not violate the Confrontation Clause of the Sixth Amendment, because the witness was unavailable by his refusal to testify at the second trial and gave his original testimony while under oath and subject to cross-examination by defendant's attorney. The court found that an instruction was improper because a reasonable juror could perceive that the burden of proof for the element of malice shifted from the state to defendant. However, the court found that the erroneous malice instruction was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt, where the jury found defendant was sane at the time of the murders and both malice and defendant's guilt were overwhelmingly supported by the evidence. The court found that defendant was not entitled to surrebuttal testimony that was merely cumulative to that which he had put forth in his case-in-chief. The court affirmed and concluded that it was not an abuse of discretion to not admit a film into evidence to explain PTSD to the jury, where the trial court concluded that the film contained a significant amount of hearsay that could not be verified by defendant's expert.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court convicting defendant of first-degree murder.

State ex rel. McDougall v. Corcoran


No. CV-86-0491-SA


Supreme Court of Arizona


153 Ariz. 157; 735 P.2d 767; 1987 Ariz. LEXIS 150



March 24, 1987 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied May 12, 1987.


PRIOR HISTORY: Maricopa County Superior Court No. LCA 27416, Phoenix City Court Nos. 5173140, 1-OC


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of driving under the influence (DUI), but found not guilty of driving with a blood alcohol content of .10 percent or more. The Maricopa County Superior Court (Arizona) reversed the DUI conviction, remanded for new trial, and denied the state's motion for reconsideration. The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: Prior to the trial in municipal court, defense counsel filed a motion in limine to preclude the prosecutor from mentioning the fact that defendant had obtained a breath sample for his own use after he was tested for the state's purposes. The municipal court denied the motion. At trial, the prosecutor elicited testimony from the arresting officer that defendant had asked for and received the sample. Later, defendant took the witness stand, and over defense objections, was asked during cross-examination whether he had received the breath sample. The superior court judge ruled that it was reversible error for the prosecutor to refer to the fact that defendant had sought and obtained a breath sample because defendant did not introduce evidence relating to the sample. The court vacated the reversal, holding that the prosecutor did not err in presenting evidence that defendant was given a breath sample or in commenting on his failure to produce evidence concerning the results. In taking the stand, defendant voluntarily subjected himself to the prosecutor's questions, and defendant had challenged the validity of the state's blood alcohol test results during the trial.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order reversing the conviction and directed reinstatement of the judgment of conviction.

Supreme Court of Arizona
